201510	526	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Let’s start with President Williams.	92	9
201510	527	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	528	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	I think this is a timely topic for two reasons.	92	9
201510	529	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	One is, I think it is useful input into our own policy decisions and deliberations in the situation we find ourselves in today.	92	9
201510	530	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	But I also think it is important for thinking about our longer-term policy strategy, and I’ll come to that in a few minutes.	92	9
201510	531	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	I found the memos and the briefings very useful and the discussion we’ve had already this morning very good, but my remarks will focus on the research on r* that Thomas Laubach and I have been working on for the past 15 years and that was summarized in the paper that we distributed to the Committee last week.	92	9
201510	532	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	And, by the way, after the paper was distributed, I received a nice e-mail from President Kocherlakota saying, and I quote, “As you think about your policy intervention for the upcoming meeting, I highly recommend this thoughtful paper.”	92	9
201510	533	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, I found it very gratifying that he found our work useful input for the Committee’s thinking.	92	9
201510	534	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	But upon closer inspection, I noticed that the e-mail was not addressed to the FOMC but only to me.	92	9
201510	535	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	[Laughter]	92	9
201510	536	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	It’s still relevant.	92	9
201510	537	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	In my reading of the literature and my own work with Thomas on this, I think a robust finding across pretty much all the studies is that r* has declined considerably over the past decade and, although point estimates differ, nearly all the current readings on r* are near historic lows.	92	9
201510	538	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	What remains unanswered is, what are the forces that have driven the natural rate of interest down?	92	9
201510	539	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Numerous explanations have been put forward, including slower economic growth, global factors, and so on, but it’s not possible to draw definitive conclusions based on the available evidence.	92	9
201510	540	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	This inability to identify clearly the sources of a decline in r* makes it hard to know how much of the shift will be temporary and how much will be long lasting.	92	9
201510	541	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Although much is made, in debate among researchers, of this distinction, for policy purposes we don’t need to wait for a definitive answer.	92	9
201510	542	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Even if the natural rate eventually returns to its higher level, it’s likely to remain low for the next few years.	92	9
201510	543	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	This is seen in the analysis from the DSGE models and, quite honestly, from all the other models in which the natural rate is assumed to be only temporarily depressed.	92	9
201510	544	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	The conclusion I draw is that a relatively low natural rate of interest is likely to be with us for some time, and we need to take that into account in our forecasts, our analysis, and our policy deliberations.	92	9
201510	545	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Particularly, it argues for a shallow path of rate increases and an endpoint for interest rates that are below historical norms.	92	9
201510	546	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	I should note that the Tealbook forecast implicitly incorporates a very low effective short-run r* and a long-run r* of only 1¼ percent.	92	9
201510	547	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	So these considerations are already built into the baseline projection to a large extent.	92	9
201510	548	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Looking further ahead, if the natural rate of interest stays persistently low, this does have direct implications for the frequency and severity of zero-lower-bound episodes and for financial stability.	92	9
201510	549	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	As prudent planning for such a possibility, we should have renewed discussions here in the Committee of the optimal size of our balance sheet, the expected use of LSAPs in the future, negative interest rates, and even the optimal inflation target and other approaches to cope with an environment of very low rates.	92	9
201510	550	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Even if you’re not convinced today that the natural rate of interest is permanently lower, I do think that it makes sense for us to focus on these issues over the next year or so.	92	9
201510	551	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you.	92	9
201510	552	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201510	553	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Rosengren.	92	9
201510	554	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	555	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	The estimate for the equilibrium federal funds rate that I submitted to the September SEP was 350 basis points.	92	9
201510	556	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	However, I must admit that I have very little confidence in my ability to estimate the real equilibrium federal funds rate in the long run or in the short run.	92	9
201510	557	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	If the next recession were to begin with the federal funds rate at 350 basis points or lower, I am worried that we would once again return to the zero lower bound.	92	9
201510	558	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Because we have lowered the federal funds rate more than 500 basis points in the wake of each of the past three recessions, the potential to hit the zero lower bound seems uncomfortably high.	92	9
201510	559	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	As the paper circulated by President Williams discusses, lower real equilibrium interest rates limit the options available to monetary policymakers to counter contractionary shocks to the economy.	92	9
201510	560	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	However, further exploring the tolerance of the Committee for expanding the balance sheet to offset average-sized recessions, assessing the effect of balance sheet actions on term premiums, and discussing the cost and benefits of a higher inflation target seem to be the most fruitful areas of discussion at this time.	92	9
201510	561	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	A lower equilibrium real interest rate implies that both the frequency and duration of zero-lower-bound episodes have risen, although it is difficult to know by how much.	92	9
201510	562	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	The past 15 years have already shown us that the frequency and duration were too high.	92	9
201510	563	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	The other implication of a lower real rate will be that we may need to respond to slowdowns with more- aggressive monetary policy actions.	92	9
201510	564	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	This may imply reducing the federal funds rate more quickly and aggressively than we have done in previous economic slowdowns, and we should prepare the public and the Congress over time for the potential that unconventional monetary policies may not be idiosyncratic, associated only with severe financial crises in the future, but may become tools more commonly deployed in economic slowdowns.	92	9
201510	565	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	566	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201510	567	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Evans.	92	9
201510	568	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	569	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	And thanks to everyone who worked on this r* project.	92	9
201510	570	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	The memos provide an excellent summary of the state of knowledge about this important topic.	92	9
201510	571	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	In fact, this topic is central to our policy deliberations.	92	9
201510	572	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	The fundamental question before the Committee this year and next is whether policy is sufficiently accommodative to support currently low inflation rising to our 2 percent objective over the medium term.	92	9
201510	573	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	These memos get to the heart of this issue.	92	9
201510	574	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Does our current zero interest rate policy represent emergency levels of monetary policy accommodation, or is it just slightly accommodative, or is it not really accommodative at all?	92	9
201510	575	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Understanding movements in r* is a way to bring all of these issues together in one place.	92	9
201510	576	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	There’s too much good material in these memos to cover all the interesting observations.	92	9
201510	577	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Here’s a few messages that I took away.	92	9
201510	578	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	First, r* has been falling over time.	92	9
201510	579	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	I think President Williams was on target there.	92	9
201510	580	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	These reductions are quite persistent.	92	9
201510	581	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Some analyses, like the DSGE models, assume stationary deviations, and they turn out to be potentially very persistent, depending on the driving process that got us there.	92	9
201510	582	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Other analyses, like Laubach-Williams, assume I(1) permanent changes that persist indefinitely.	92	9
201510	583	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	I agree with President Kocherlakota’s e- mail to you quite a lot.	92	9
201510	584	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	[Laughter]	92	9
201510	585	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	The most recent Laubach-Williams r* estimates are close to zero and permanent.	92	9
201510	586	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	A very low r* combined with our 2 percent inflation objective implies that dealing with the zero lower bound will continue to be a big risk even once we get inflation back to target.	92	9
201510	587	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	So it’s important that we know how to deal with future situations when our policy rate may be forced to zero.	92	9
201510	588	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	I agree with all of the suggestions that we should continue to study our tools that we could bring to bear on that situation when we find ourselves at the effective lower bound again.	92	9
201510	589	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Second, even though it is not totally clear why real rates have been falling, it seems to be a worldwide phenomenon that has been going on for two or three decades.	92	9
201510	590	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	I agree with Kei-Mu and Jing that most of the developments that we can foresee suggest r* is going to stay low or maybe fall even lower, leaving us in a world in which the effective lower bound may be frequently binding.	92	9
201510	591	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Third, with r* currently close to zero, we cannot just assume that today’s zero funds rate is providing large amounts of policy accommodation.	92	9
201510	592	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	It appears that we are only below the equilibrium nominal rate by something on the order of 150 basis points.	92	9
201510	593	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Some have characterized our current policy rates as being at an emergency setting, a comment that’s clearly meant to imply that emergency rates are no longer appropriate for current economic conditions.	92	9
201510	594	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	My view from these memos is that having the real funds rate about 150 basis points below r* is not really an emergency setting.	92	9
201510	595	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Maintaining a zero funds rate is just prudent policy following a long period when we would have liked to have put rates much, much lower but couldn’t because of the effective lower bound.	92	9
201510	596	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	These low r* estimates also mean that two or three quarter-point increases in the funds rate could eliminate a good portion of the accommodation that we are finally now able to provide.	92	9
201510	597	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Fourth, uncertainty about r* most likely implies that “lower for longer” is a prudent risk- management policy.	92	9
201510	598	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	It’s not enough to simply point to wide uncertainty bands surrounding r* estimates.	92	9
201510	599	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	We need to assess the variety of policy losses under different realizations of r*.	92	9
201510	600	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Our policy losses are asymmetric at the zero lower bound, and, hence, in David, Chris, Ben, and Bob’s memo, optimal policy delays liftoff and prescribes lower rates until uncertainty about r* and the odds of hitting the effective lower bound are substantially reduced.	92	9
201510	601	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	My Brookings paper with Fisher, Gourio, and Krane reached a similar conclusion on the basis of a different information structure.	92	9
201510	602	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Finally, President Lacker brought up an interesting point and criticism of our monetary policies related to—I can’t really penetrate these models very well—the New Monetarist models with alternative equilibrium calculations.	92	9
201510	603	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	I think that if you go out in public, you can hear people make comments like this to us.	92	9
201510	604	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	I think it might be worth our time to figure out what is there and what is not.	92	9
201510	605	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Every time I hear the Fisher equation mentioned as an important point in this regard, I’ve come away with the impression that it’s the slavish appeal to an interest rate rule that interacts with the Fisher equation to validate this equilibrium, and it’s really quite fragile.	92	9
201510	606	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	If you were to break from that interest rate rule, and maybe you’ve got a threshold that tells you, “When I get way off target, I break from it,” that’s going to break that equilibrium.	92	9
201510	607	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	These are kind of transversality-type conditions.	92	9
201510	608	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	President Bullard, some years ago, when he was talking about this issue, similarly argued that maybe we should start doing more asset purchases, work the money angle on this.	92	9
201510	609	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	That’s another attempt to break the slavish commitment to that interest rate rule, and that knocks that equilibrium out.	92	9
201510	610	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	It’s outside the model, but in talking about these things, as President Lacker mentioned, if the public starts thinking we’re going after lower inflation, it begins to ratify this.	92	9
201510	611	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	That’s why I think it’s so important for us to recommit ourselves firmly to our inflation objective of 2 percent in an aggressive symmetric approach to that so that people won’t be left with that wrong impression and perhaps validate that type of outcome.	92	9
201510	612	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	613	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201510	614	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Vice Chairman.	92	9
201510	615	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	616	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	And thanks to the staff for these memos.	92	9
201510	617	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	They are very thoughtful and stimulating.	92	9
201510	618	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	When I think about r*, I have two concepts in mind, one short run and one long run.	92	9
201510	619	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The short-run r* is consistent with what I think is a neutral monetary policy today, and the long-run r* is what I think will be consistent with neutrality in the long run.	92	9
201510	620	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I don’t have a precise estimate of what either of those are, but I certainly agree that both are likely to be well below what we have seen historically.	92	9
201510	621	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	With respect to the short-run r*, I would put some weight on the model results, but I also would look elsewhere—simply, how fast has the economy been growing?	92	9
201510	622	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	If r* were really close to 2 percent, I doubt we’d be growing at only a slightly above-trend pace right now.	92	9
201510	623	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I think we also have to be a bit careful and not overemphasize the DSGE model results too much because these models typically have very underdeveloped financial sectors, if they have one at all.	92	9
201510	624	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	This relevant in the current environment, because I think developments in the financial system have been important in depressing the real equilibrium rate.	92	9
201510	625	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	It’s not just about longer-term fundamentals such as productivity growth and demographic trends.	92	9
201510	626	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	For myself, I think that short-run r* has been held down by strict lending standards for mortgages, especially for households with low FICO scores.	92	9
201510	627	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Also, to the extent we have had higher capital requirements and other regulations, they have weighed on the intermediation margin.	92	9
201510	628	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I would expect those would push down r* slightly, both now and in the longer run.	92	9
201510	629	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I think memories of the crisis may also be important through their effects on precautionary saving, less borrowing.	92	9
201510	630	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Presumably, that influence will diminish over time, assuming we don’t have any new crises.	92	9
201510	631	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	As I have noted in speeches for some time, I also think the appropriate stance of policy does depend on how financial conditions evolve because monetary policy is not transmitted directly to the real economy but, instead, through its effect on financial conditions.	92	9
201510	632	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I think this is particularly important because the linkage between short-term rates and broader financial conditions is not stable through time.	92	9
201510	633	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The events of the fall of 2008 are the most noteworthy example of that.	92	9
201510	634	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Financial conditions tightened abruptly, and this called for a much lower short- term interest rate and response.	92	9
201510	635	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	That implies that the level of short-run r* is affected by financial conditions and how they respond to cyclical, secular, and even political developments.	92	9
201510	636	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	My expectation about the short-run r* is that it will gradually rise over time as some of the financial headwinds generated by the financial crisis fade.	92	9
201510	637	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I would expect that household credit histories and FICO scores will gradually improve as we get further away from the Great Recession.	92	9
201510	638	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Also, as home prices recover, the number of mortgages that are underwater should decline.	92	9
201510	639	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	This may also lead to a greater access to credit for affected households.	92	9
201510	640	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	At the same time, I don’t expect r* to get all the way back to 2 percent in the long run.	92	9
201510	641	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	So I’m with President Rosengren, 3½ percent on the long-run nominal federal funds rate without a lot of conviction.	92	9
201510	642	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	But it strikes me that an aging population, slower labor force growth, and somewhat slower productivity growth should all be factors that suggest that r* will stay below 2 percent for the foreseeable future.	92	9
201510	643	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Finally, I think it’s important that we don’t overfixate on r* compared with other issues.	92	9
201510	644	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	For example, this issue of how far we want the policy rate to deviate from our short-term r* estimate is also important.	92	9
201510	645	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Presumably, there is a path back to full employment and price stability that is superior to others in terms of its welfare-maximizing attributes.	92	9
201510	646	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	We need to be open-minded about the size of the differential that we should be seeking and not just accept the prescriptions as simple policy rules because those policy rules seem to be doing a good job of describing monetary policy in the past.	92	9
201510	647	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The impetus to economic growth from a given gap between the policy rate and r* might be different now than in the past.	92	9
201510	648	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	And, if that was the case, then that should affect our choice of the policy rate and the size of the gap.	92	9
201510	649	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Also, I would argue that, currently, risk-management concerns may imply a wider differential than maybe we have had in the past.	92	9
201510	650	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	651	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201510	652	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Harker.	92	9
201510	653	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	654	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	In reflecting on the memo and the presentation, I just have two brief comments related not to the theory but to our current policy decision, what faces us right now.	92	9
201510	655	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	First, I’m on board with the general thrust of the memos that the neutral rate is zero to slightly positive.	92	9
201510	656	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	This implies to me that the policy is currently too accommodative.	92	9
201510	657	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	And I believe this view is broadly consistent with the unemployment rate being close to its natural rate.	92	9
201510	658	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	Second, I think that the steady-state r* is probably around 1.75 percent.	92	9
201510	659	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	The US economy is fundamentally sound, and productivity should pick up in the future.	92	9
201510	660	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	Because of the after-effects of the financial crisis, neutral r* is below its steady-state value.	92	9
201510	661	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	But it will rise over time, and monetary policy should keep pace with that rise.	92	9
201510	662	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	Additionally, and I think this is important, we need to learn, and we will be learning, about r* as normalization proceeds on a shallow path.	92	9
201510	663	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	And we need to see how the economy evolves as interest rates rise.	92	9
201510	664	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	There is an experiment that we will be conducting, and it’s an important experiment, to watch how r* evolves as we move along this shallow path.	92	9
201510	665	2015-10-28	meeting	HARKER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	666	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201510	667	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Kocherlakota.	92	9
201510	668	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	669	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I want to thank all of the presenters and the authors for a great set of memos.	92	9
201510	670	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I find the analysis to be highly informative, as I believe I will illustrate through my comments.	92	9
201510	671	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And, as others have noted, I think this is a very timely topic, in terms not only of the current policy considerations facing the Committee but also in terms of the policy considerations that may be facing the Committee for years to come.	92	9
201510	672	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I have three high-level takeaways from the memo.	92	9
201510	673	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Short-run r* has risen from recessionary lows, and there are good reasons to expect short-run r* to continue to rise.	92	9
201510	674	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	However, there have been significant downward trends in long-run and steady-state r*.	92	9
201510	675	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If those trends were to persist, we should expect short-run r* to remain below its 2007 level for years to come.	92	9
201510	676	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This is broadly consistent with the pattern that we see in longer-term TIPS yields.	92	9
201510	677	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There are some issues there about sorting out term premiums from the behavior of expectations of short-run real interest rates, but it is broadly consistent with what we see in longer-term TIPS yields.	92	9
201510	678	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There are many uncertainties associated with the preceding two statements.	92	9
201510	679	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In general, because of the presence of the effective lower bound, those uncertainties argue for more- accommodative policy.	92	9
201510	680	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My summary of these takeaways is that it would be appropriate for the FOMC to use a reaction function, both now and in the future, that has a lower intercept, potentially much lower, than the familiar Taylor rules.	92	9
201510	681	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I want to make two additional comments.	92	9
201510	682	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The first concerns a potentially important takeaway from the memos about the nature of the slope coefficients in the FOMC policy reaction function.	92	9
201510	683	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We focused a lot of the discussion on the intercepts through our conversation about r*.	92	9
201510	684	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The second comment I want to make builds on President Mester’s questions about low r* and measures of financial stability.	92	9
201510	685	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My first comment is about the nature of the slope coefficients in the FOMC policy reaction function.	92	9
201510	686	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The questions at the end of the memo, which I’ll note that most of my colleagues have neatly sidestepped, are about our assessments of r*, with the idea that we could plug those different assessments into a policy rule.	92	9
201510	687	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I have heard from the comments made by others around the table—President Williams, Vice Chairman Dudley—it is pretty hard to know what is driving r*.	92	9
201510	688	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And so I think it is going to be pretty hard to formulate and communicate measures of r* on an ongoing basis.	92	9
201510	689	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, I think the memo by López-Salido and others was potentially helpful along these lines.	92	9
201510	690	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It points out that if we use a Taylor rule with a high coefficient on inflation deviations— and Thomas pointed out that this is a lesson from President Williams’s former work as well—we can substitute for this kind of uncertainty while essentially using a policy rule that tracks variations in r* around its long-run value, without having to measure those variations.	92	9
201510	691	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And the intuition behind this finding, I think, is simple to understand when the only shocks present are to the natural real rate of interest.	92	9
201510	692	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So there are no shocks to the Phillips curve.	92	9
201510	693	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In that case, it is optimal to use a rule that tracks the natural real rate of interest, this so-called “divine coincidence” case.	92	9
201510	694	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If you don’t have shocks to the Phillips curve, you’re going to get divine coincidence.	92	9
201510	695	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Another way to say that, though, is that it’s always optimal to tighten further if inflation is too high and to ease further if inflation is too low.	92	9
201510	696	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That suggests we can get good outcomes by using a rule that has a high coefficient on inflation gaps.	92	9
201510	697	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In a recent working paper, Chris House of the University of Michigan and a student coauthor show that we can get arbitrarily close to desirable outcomes in this no-shocks-to-the-Phillips-curve case by using a rule that has arbitrarily large coefficients on inflation.	92	9
201510	698	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So you don’t have to measure the variations in r* around its long-term value, you just have to track inflation and respond to inflation very aggressively.	92	9
201510	699	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think this is potentially an important lesson.	92	9
201510	700	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As we all talked about already, measuring r* and communicating about it could be very hard.	92	9
201510	701	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Responding a lot more aggressively to inflation is not.	92	9
201510	702	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think it’s easy to communicate to the public why you might want to do that.	92	9
201510	703	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s true that inflation isn’t perfectly measured, and this is an important consideration that drives us, for example, to talk about core inflation in a lot of our communications.	92	9
201510	704	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But we could filter it by using forecast inflation instead.	92	9
201510	705	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This was, indeed, a favorite prescription of Chairman Bernanke, and it is also very similar to the goal-oriented approach that President Evans and I have advocated for some time—that you put a lot of weight on your goals, and that drives what your setting of the policy rate would be.	92	9
201510	706	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So that’s my first comment.	92	9
201510	707	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Results in the memo suggest we can track variations in r* around its long-run value, if you were to use a rule that was more aggressive, potentially a lot more aggressive—the memo by López-Salido and others settles on a coefficient of 20—about responding to actual or forecast inflation gaps.	92	9
201510	708	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My second comment follows up on what President Mester was saying about financial stability.	92	9
201510	709	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I did a search of the papers for the words “financial stability,” and it came up blank.	92	9
201510	710	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I didn’t find a single reference to financial stability.	92	9
201510	711	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think this is an important omission that I hope future work will fill.	92	9
201510	712	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	About two and a half years ago, I gave a speech about low r* and financial stability, and I pointed out that if r* is to be low permanently or even high persistently, we are only going to be able to achieve our dual-mandate objectives in the presence of signs of financial stability.	92	9
201510	713	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	President Williams, at our previous meeting, expressed concern that housing markets, at least in California, or potentially in the nation as a whole, were unduly frothy because rent-to-price ratios had fallen back to 2003 levels.	92	9
201510	714	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But if r* is going to be low by previous historical standards for years to come, then we are only going to achieve our dual-mandate objectives if things like the rent-to-price ratio are indeed low by previous historical standards.	92	9
201510	715	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My own thinking is that Kei-Mu and Jing are right, that there are good reasons to believe that r* is likely to be low for years to come.	92	9
201510	716	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If so, it will be even more important for central banks to integrate financial-stability considerations into their policy assessments.	92	9
201510	717	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The QS analysis is a valuable first step along those lines for this Committee, and I will talk about some ways that I hope we can build on it.	92	9
201510	718	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Maybe you can build on that analysis in the next go-round.	92	9
201510	719	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I thought there already were some very thoughtful comments—I will just echo that I agree with them very strongly—that President Williams and President Rosengren made.	92	9
201510	720	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If r* is going to be low for quite some time to come, that really suggests the need to reevaluate the whole framework of the Committee.	92	9
201510	721	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Are things like LSAPs an emergency tool, a break-the- glass kind of tool, or something that really should be part of the toolkit if r* is going to remain low?	92	9
201510	722	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Contemplating negative interest rates has certainly proven to be useful in Europe as well as potentially contemplating a change in the inflation target itself.	92	9
201510	723	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	All of these, I think, are things for the Committee to think about if, in fact, r* is going to prove to be low for a long time to come.	92	9
201510	724	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But you’ll get some information about that, as President Harker pointed out.	92	9
201510	725	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thanks a lot.	92	9
201510	726	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201510	727	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Powell.	92	9
201510	728	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	729	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	I have just a couple of brief comments.	92	9
201510	730	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	According to this excellent staff work, as well as many other estimates and under a variety of definitions, r* has declined over two or three decades to a very low level, now about zero, with no signs yet of recovery.	92	9
201510	731	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	And the markets and a variety of factors suggest that a low r* is likely to be with us for a while.	92	9
201510	732	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	What are the implications of this?	92	9
201510	733	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	In the short term, while r* may be low, short-term rates are now substantially negative.	92	9
201510	734	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	So real short-term rates are providing a good deal of stimulus.	92	9
201510	735	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	For me, that’s appropriate for three reasons: We have inflation below mandate; we have economic growth that is barely above trend; and we have, as page 10 of the handout shows, five years to make up for when the Committee was unable, probably despite significant unconventional monetary policy, to provide adequate support for the economy.	92	9
201510	736	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	For me, the implications of the precise timing of liftoff are more ambiguous.	92	9
201510	737	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	I agree that uncertainty regarding r* argues for some inertia when policymakers are at the zero lower bound.	92	9
201510	738	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	Indeed, there are many arguments that support the idea of having inertia at the zero lower bound.	92	9
201510	739	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	But what is the right amount of inertia or patience?	92	9
201510	740	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	Is it more or less than the Committee has already employed?	92	9
201510	741	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	This work, to me, doesn’t speak directly to that question.	92	9
201510	742	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	There is also the argument that uncertainty about the level of r* as well as about the level of the natural rate of unemployment should lead us to de-emphasize those factors in setting policy, which tends to lead, then, to something that focuses on the inflation gap, which we are better at observing, and also the change in the output gap, or a first-difference rule.	92	9
201510	743	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	And I would say that the first- difference rule is also consistent with liftoff in the current time frame.	92	9
201510	744	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	So I don’t take any obvious signal about the precise timing of liftoff from this work.	92	9
201510	745	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	I would say that the medium- and longer-term implications of a sustained low r* are clearer and more troubling.	92	9
201510	746	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	If economic growth is going to continue along just a bit above its lackluster trend rate, it would not surprise me if every single rate decision will be very challenging in the coming years.	92	9
201510	747	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	On that growth path, it is going to be extraordinarily difficult— indeed, it would be inappropriate—to raise rates at anything other than a very gradual pace.	92	9
201510	748	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	And, over time, as others have stressed, that very low path implies more frequent and longer episodes at the effective lower bound.	92	9
201510	749	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	On that last point, I read one of Andy Haldane’s clever speeches over the summer titled “Stuck,” which caused me to get into a series of discussions with Bill English and many others.	92	9
201510	750	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	And Bill ran some numbers, which I’ll share.	92	9
201510	751	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	Based on historical data, experience suggests that the chance of a recession over the next four years from today is about 40 or 50 percent.	92	9
201510	752	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	The Committee has typically cut rates about 400 basis points, give or take 100 basis points, in a recession.	92	9
201510	753	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	So, if you take the market’s projected path for rates, or even the latest primary dealer survey, you will see that almost any recession for the next several years will return us to zero interest rates.	92	9
201510	754	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	So I would echo what others have said around the table.	92	9
201510	755	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	There really is a need to evaluate all of our tools, including forward guidance, the balance sheet, and negative rates, because much as one might hate to admit it, we may very well need them.	92	9
201510	756	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	In fact, we are likely to need some or all of those tools.	92	9
201510	757	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	The other thing is that a gradual increase in r* to more normal levels is a fundamental assumption underlying the Tealbook forecast for interest rates.	92	9
201510	758	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	And, if this work calls that assumption into question, it really does suggest that the set of issues regarding this question will be the major challenge facing monetary policy in the coming years.	92	9
201510	759	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	760	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201510	761	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Bullard.	92	9
201510	762	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	763	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I have many comments on the r* memos.	92	9
201510	764	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Congratulations to the staff on putting together an excellent analysis of this important issue.	92	9
201510	765	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I’ll give you a summary, and then I’ll provide more detail.	92	9
201510	766	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The summary is this.	92	9
201510	767	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	My first comment is that there is a lot of uncertainty about concepts and estimates, and that suggests caution.	92	9
201510	768	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I’ll talk about that a little bit.	92	9
201510	769	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	My second comment is that marginal product of capital is higher and smoother since 1985 than the declining returns on government paper.	92	9
201510	770	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I would take that marginal product of capital to be the more relevant rate, so I’ll talk about that a little bit.	92	9
201510	771	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I’ll talk about the simple New Keynesian model, which was used a lot here in the memo.	92	9
201510	772	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	That model has no capital.	92	9
201510	773	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	It also predicts mean reversion, as we have been talking about already this morning, so I think there are two strikes against that particular model and the policy implications coming out of that.	92	9
201510	774	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I’ll talk a little bit about the Laubach- Williams model-based r* estimate.	92	9
201510	775	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I’d prefer an independent measurement of real returns.	92	9
201510	776	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	On policy, I have several comments.	92	9
201510	777	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	For r* itself, I am going to suggest sticking with constants or modifying the constants only slightly.	92	9
201510	778	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	These are, after all, 25-year trends that we are talking about in a lot of this work.	92	9
201510	779	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I’m also going to say that adopting radical values for r* would require a rhetorical shift on the Committee that I’m not sure we’re really prepared for.	92	9
201510	780	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	We can talk about that a little bit.	92	9
201510	781	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The real returns to government paper have clearly declined, suggesting a safe asset shortage.	92	9
201510	782	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I think the policy implications of that are unclear.	92	9
201510	783	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	And, finally, the permanence of the Laubach-Williams estimate suggests possible convergence to a low nominal interest rate–low inflation steady state delineated by Benhabib, Schmitt-Grohé, and Uribe, which has also already been discussed this morning.	92	9
201510	784	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I have a lot of comments.	92	9
201510	785	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I apologize for that.	92	9
201510	786	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	But I just wanted to get my thoughts on the table here.	92	9
201510	787	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Let’s take these one by one.	92	9
201510	788	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	First, regarding uncertainty about the concepts and estimates, the memos clearly indicated that there are a lot of ideas about theoretical concepts relevant to r* and that these concepts are used in different ways by different authors.	92	9
201510	789	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Empirical estimates also vary widely and use different versions of the underlying theoretical constructs.	92	9
201510	790	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The initial table explaining the issue has a myriad of different definitions.	92	9
201510	791	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	This, of course, leads to considerable uncertainty in interpreting empirical results and the associated policy advice.	92	9
201510	792	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	So the first comment is just that we should be quite careful about relying too heavily on new interpretations of r* as a driver of US monetary policy.	92	9
201510	793	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The second comment concerns marginal product of capital versus the returns to government debt.	92	9
201510	794	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	A lot of the empirical results contained in these memos are essentially stating that the real rate of return on government paper today is very low by historical standards.	92	9
201510	795	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The real rate of return to capital, as calculated by Gomme, Ravikumar, and Rupert, and by others, tends to be higher and shows little or no downward trend since 1985.	92	9
201510	796	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The marginal product of capital may be the more relevant concept for real macroeconomic performance, and it is not indicating substantially different behavior from postwar history.	92	9
201510	797	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The high price and corresponding low real returns of government paper suggest that the idea that there is “a shortage of safe assets” globally may have considerable merit.	92	9
201510	798	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	This idea has been analyzed by authors like Caballero and Farhi as well as Andolfatto and Williamson.	92	9
201510	799	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	In these papers, government debt has liquidity value, collateral value, or both.	92	9
201510	800	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	But the policy implications are far from fully settled, and we would need quite a bit more work to put a lot of weight on that.	92	9
201510	801	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The third set of comments concerns the simple New Keynesian models.	92	9
201510	802	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The results on the marginal product of capital versus returns to government debt call into question the policy advice in the memo stemming from the use of the simple New Keynesian model alone.	92	9
201510	803	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	As useful as that model is, there is no capital in the model.	92	9
201510	804	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	If there was capital, its return might be expected to be falling in tandem with the returns on government paper over the past 25 years, which is arguably not happening in the data.	92	9
201510	805	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	In addition, the simple New Keynesian model has a unique steady-state equilibrium, so the model predicts mean reversion of r* to its long-run value.	92	9
201510	806	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	As Laubach and Williams point out, comically but also tragically, in their figure 10, repeated predictions of an imminent rise in r* have turned out to be incorrect during the past six years.	92	9
201510	807	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The time scale over which the Laubach-Williams estimate of a low r* value is beyond ordinary business-cycle frequencies, possibly indicating, instead, convergence to a low nominal interest rate–low inflation steady state.	92	9
201510	808	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	In the fourth set of comments, Laubach and Williams take a longer-run view in their paper and use multivariate estimation with a common filter to interpret recent US inflation behavior as suggesting that potential output must be on a lower path than previously believed and, therefore, that r* must be lower.	92	9
201510	809	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The nature of this analysis is summarized in the outstanding quote from the 1931 QJE article by John H.	92	9
201510	810	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Williams, affectionately known as “H” among the Williams economists.	92	9
201510	811	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	[Laughter]	92	9
201510	812	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	In a nutshell, r* is calculated under the null hypothesis that the model is true.	92	9
201510	813	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Although I think this is an interesting exercise, I would prefer to see independent estimates concerning the relevant rates of return, such as marginal product of capital.	92	9
201510	814	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	For comments on policy, the first comment concerns constants in the intercept term.	92	9
201510	815	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The original Taylor rule-type analyses have constants for r*.	92	9
201510	816	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Much of the usefulness and intuition behind the success of the Taylor rule is based on this type of formulation.	92	9
201510	817	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	It is far from clear that the use of a time-varying version would have worked better over the postwar era.	92	9
201510	818	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The spirit of the original analysis was that the real returns most relevant to economic growth and development, like the marginal product of capital, are relatively smooth and well approximated by a constant.	92	9
201510	819	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Ideas about real rates based on consumption growth or TFP plus population growth also provide relatively smooth measures, which might be viewed as consistent with the original Taylor rule analysis.	92	9
201510	820	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	My advice on this dimension is that we stick with constants or modify them only slightly.	92	9
201510	821	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	A second comment on policy:  Taking on a more radical interpretation of r*, an estimate like the one reported by Vasco Cúrdia, who is at the Federal Reserve Bank of San Francisco, which is minus 2 percent, would imply that US monetary policy is currently not accommodative.	92	9
201510	822	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	At least that would be one interpretation.	92	9
201510	823	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	This would require, in my opinion, a dramatic shift in Committee rhetoric, which has repeatedly emphasized “highly accommodative” policy over the past five years.	92	9
201510	824	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	This is probably far too dramatic a shift in rhetoric to be credible.	92	9
201510	825	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I think if we wanted to go in that direction, we could do it, but we have told people that we are aiding the recovery, that we have done all we can, we have supplemented zero rates with many other actions, promises to stay at zero longer, and quantitative easing.	92	9
201510	826	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	So we have said that we have a highly accommodative policy.	92	9
201510	827	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I think it would be a little bit jarring to say, “Well, you know, it’s not that accommodative after all.”	92	9
201510	828	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	We could make that argument, but it would certainly be a shock, maybe to ourselves and to how people outside this Committee have been thinking about monetary policy over the past five or six years.	92	9
201510	829	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The third comment on policy is that, while the marginal product of capital has arguably held relatively steady, real returns to government debt have declined for many years.	92	9
201510	830	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	This suggests that the “shortage of safe assets” story is a promising direction for future thinking on monetary policy.	92	9
201510	831	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Policy implications are unsettled.	92	9
201510	832	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The last comment on policy is that the permanence of the decline in real returns to government debt and related assets suggests the possibility of convergence to a new steady state, as analyzed by Benhabib and others.	92	9
201510	833	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	If that is the case, then promising an even longer time at the zero bound, directly or through quantitative easing, is not doing anything except reinforcing the BSU steady state.	92	9
201510	834	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201510	835	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201510	836	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	A two-hander from President Kocherlakota.	92	9
201510	837	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I wanted to comment on President Bullard’s point about the rhetoric from the Committee about accommodation.	92	9
201510	838	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think, certainly, the Committee has pointed out that we have used a lot of historically unprecedented forms of accommodation given the fact that we are at the zero lower bound.	92	9
201510	839	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That’s what I always took the rhetoric about high levels of accommodation to mean—that our tools, because of the evolution of the economy, had to be historically unprecedented.	92	9
201510	840	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I do not think that the American public believes that the economy has evolved according to what would be deemed as being appropriate over the past five or six years.	92	9
201510	841	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I don’t think it will come as a shock for them to find out that inflation has been below target for seven years, or that output has been growing so slowly.	92	9
201510	842	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I don’t think it would take a major change in communications, simply more focusing on the outcomes of our policy on inflation and output.	92	9
201510	843	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That’s what gives rise to calculations like Cúrdia’s.	92	9
201510	844	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Brainard.	92	9
201510	845	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I will follow up on this conversation about communications.	92	9
201510	846	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I think the very helpful work that the staff presented to us is quite illuminating for how we think about as well as how we talk about what we mean by “normalization.”	92	9
201510	847	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I went to our website, and it equates normalization with steps to raise the federal funds rate and other short-term interest rates to more normal levels.	92	9
201510	848	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	But it begs the question, what are more normal levels?	92	9
201510	849	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	It clearly implies that the target range for the federal funds rate is currently below normal.	92	9
201510	850	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	We haven’t, I don’t think, explicitly defined what we mean by “normal,” but the central tendency, or the median, of the path in the SEP might reasonably be considered a rough guide.	92	9
201510	851	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	And by that proxy, the definition of normal has moved down noticeably at all horizons over the course of the Committee’s discussion of what normal is.	92	9
201510	852	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I think today’s discussion, and the work that we do based on it, does provide a common analytical framework that should help us get to a more common definition of what we have in mind with “normal.”	92	9
201510	853	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	It also perhaps can help educate the public, which I think is very important.	92	9
201510	854	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I think what stands out from the work that was presented here, from the empirical estimates of the shorter-run natural rate of interest from the DSGE models and the other important work that has been done in this area, is that a wide variety of models suggest that r* has been negative in recent years and is only now approaching or is close to zero six years into the recovery.	92	9
201510	855	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	With these estimates far below the average level in previous decades, it implies that the accommodation associated with any given level of the federal funds rate has fallen short of what one might have been thinking about in historical terms.	92	9
201510	856	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	And those shorter-run estimates imply that the current level of the federal funds rate is likely to be mildly accommodative, something that might help explain the very gradual pace of expansion in activity and, in particular, despite being low by historical standards, a very long period of low rates has coincided with only modest growth in investment throughout the recovery.	92	9
201510	857	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	There are, I think, a host of observable reasons that the natural rate might be low currently.	92	9
201510	858	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	And, of course, earlier in the recovery, the dominant explanations focused on domestic factors and headwinds from the crisis.	92	9
201510	859	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	More recently, I think it’s likely that the foreign outlook has become increasingly important as a source of downward pressure on short-run r*.	92	9
201510	860	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Weak economic growth trajectories are now the norm in much of the world.	92	9
201510	861	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	And, as we know, in the other G-7 economies we have seen a very large shift down in 10-year sovereign bond yields, a shift that has been persistent over the past year or so.	92	9
201510	862	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	But also, interestingly, if we look at emerging markets, we have seen a broad-based reduction in interest rates with average real Treasury bill rates falling from about 5 percent earlier to around zero in recent times.	92	9
201510	863	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The way this would spill over into our rates would be through adjustments in the exchange rate.	92	9
201510	864	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I think the staff provided a useful simulation to get a sense of how big an adjustment in the federal funds rate might be necessary to restore monetary policy to “normal” in the sense of insulating domestic employment from an appreciation in the dollar.	92	9
201510	865	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	So let’s just take a 10 percent appreciation of the dollar, which is the experiment that they ran, which is about two-thirds of the movement that we have seen in the past year alone.	92	9
201510	866	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	It suggests that in the context of a Taylor (1999) rule with a fixed intercept, normalization of monetary policy in the sense of insulating us from this exchange rate shock would call for lowering the intercept about 60 basis points.	92	9
201510	867	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	And because we’re in the neighborhood of the effective lower bound, that means that you push off liftoff as well as pull the path down.	92	9
201510	868	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Another benchmark we use for determining what constitutes normal is the medium-term FRB/US r*.	92	9
201510	869	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	And there we have seen that, by that metric, the current stance of policy is quite accommodative relative to what would be prescribed by that, which is the level of the funds rate that, if maintained, would close the output gap in 12 quarters.	92	9
201510	870	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	What is important, I think, is that that benchmark actually takes no account of inflation.	92	9
201510	871	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	If you look at that benchmark, it gets us to 1 percent above where we should be on the output gap, and we are still shy of our 2 percent inflation target at the end of the 12 quarters, suggesting that if we cared a lot about inflation, we would actually want a more accommodative stance.	92	9
201510	872	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Or, similarly, we are pretty far along in the recovery, and if we instead wanted to think about policy normalization as closing the output gap in, say, 4 quarters instead of 12 quarters, again, that benchmark regarding what constitutes normal would suggest a lower federal funds rate would be appropriate right now.	92	9
201510	873	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I think the discussion around the table has also been good at focusing on several factors that suggest that the medium-run or even long-run natural rate of interest has also shifted down.	92	9
201510	874	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	And for reasons that many people around the table have talked about, and that resonate a great deal with me, if we believe there has been such a shift down, we would want to think very seriously about what that implies for the likelihood that we see the policy rate returning to the effective lower bound with more frequency and with longer duration than has been the case historically.	92	9
201510	875	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	And, in that context, risk-management considerations and the asymmetry of them, I think, would lead one to counsel a cautious stance even today.	92	9
201510	876	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Finally, I think the likely low level of the natural rate of interest in today’s conditions, and the implications for what can be considered normal monetary policy, do suggest we should be thinking very much about our communications and devoting some care and attention to how we talk about what “normal” means and is likely to mean as we go forward.	92	9
201510	877	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I think it is very striking that there is a draft bill in the Congress that would codify a 2 percent intercept in a Taylor equation as the benchmark by which monetary policy would be evaluated at a time when most researchers believe the empirical regularities that underpinned that relationship are unlikely to hold.	92	9
201510	878	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I think several around the table have also suggested that it may lead us to want to reevaluate unconventional tools and consider whether they will need to be used more frequently.	92	9
201510	879	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	If that is the case, we should really start communicating that now, because I believe there are much higher bars for returning to a realm of unconventional tools than there are for simple adjustments of the interest rate up and down.	92	9
201510	880	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9

201510	884	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	Like everyone else, I would like to thank the authors for their very good work, and I would add to their four papers, the paper sent to the FOMC by John Williams, one of those he wrote with Thomas Laubach, “Measuring the Natural Rate of Interest Redux.”	92	9
201510	885	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	I was all ready to summarize everything, and then I looked at exhibit 13 and realized that they had copied what I was going to say and put it into their presentation.	92	9
201510	886	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	It’s on page 13 of the handout, “Equilibrium Real Interest Rates.”	92	9
201510	887	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	I think that it is a good a statement of what the overall findings are in the very interesting work that we have been presented.	92	9
201510	888	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	Obviously, the finding that everyone knows is that the natural rate of interest has fallen sharply and shows few signs of recovering.	92	9
201510	889	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	That’s happened since the start of the Great Recession, and it is a common finding among all of the papers.	92	9
201510	890	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	There is, I think, another key point, and here I’m going to diverge a little from what everyone has been talking about.	92	9
201510	891	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	The point is that r* also depends on a set of other variables, some of which are policy-related—for example, fiscal policy; productivity growth, I don’t know to what extent it is policy-related; the real exchange rate, which is monetary policy-related; the relative price of energy; and demographics.	92	9
201510	892	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	All of these are factors affecting r*, and I think we need to start talking about what it is that moved r* more than using r* as a shorthand way of saying, “We’ve got to keep the interest rate very low.”	92	9
201510	893	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	I say that for two reasons.	92	9
201510	894	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	I think we need to speak up much more about the policy responsibilities of other agencies in this town, including the fiscal authorities, which I know we are not allowed to do, but I am never quite sure why.	92	9
201510	895	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	All other central banks do it.	92	9
201510	896	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	And I suspect that productivity growth will have far more influence on future r* than what it is that we do with the interest rate.	92	9
201510	897	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	The interest rate works through investment.	92	9
201510	898	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	Since Solow’s initial paper, there hasn’t been another paper that says technical change is not the main driver of economic growth.	92	9
201510	899	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	And I suspect we will end up there one day.	92	9
201510	900	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	I have been trying to figure out what is the use of r*.	92	9
201510	901	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	You read Wicksell, and it is either brilliantly simple or it’s too simple.	92	9
201510	902	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	It says there is a marginal rate of return on capital.	92	9
201510	903	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	And when the interest rate is below that, then there is going to be a high level of investment and more inflation.	92	9
201510	904	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	And when the marginal product of capital is below the interest rate, then we are going to get deflation.	92	9
201510	905	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	And so there is a rate which will keep the price level constant.	92	9
201510	906	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	Well, that’s what we calculate in our models, and we get a measure of r* that takes us back to a steady state of some sort over 12 quarters, and that’s what we use.	92	9
201510	907	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	But why don’t we just use the models?	92	9
201510	908	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	What is the extra that we get out of the r* as opposed to what we get out of the different models we consult when we are looking at different policies, which would take us to where we want to go?	92	9
201510	909	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	It was very interesting that one of the findings was that, using r* instead of a calculated optimal policy, you could actually do fairly well.	92	9

201510	947	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	We are now more recently, since the crisis, on the flip side of that debt supercycle, and we are having to deal with deleveraging, which I assume has to bring down r*.	92	9
