201510	1552	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	I’m going to comment on these charts from a slightly different perspective as we sit here in approximately year six since the post-2009 low.	FS2	
201510	1553	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	I agree with your conclusions.	FS2	
201510	1554	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	I actually see a number of markets that seem to me to be quite overvalued.	FS2	
201510	1555	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	The US stock market leads the list for me when you consider that corporate earnings for 2015 are estimated to be down year-over-year and the market is still trading at about 19 times 2015 earnings.	FS2	
201510	1556	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	It’s one thing for the market to be trading at 17 or 18 times earnings when earnings are rising, but these earnings are actually declining.	FS2	
201510	1557	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	This multiple really strikes me as full.	FS2	
201510	1558	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Having said that, unlike a so-called bubble in which you have severe overvaluation that could lead to a boom and a bust, what I see that worries me maybe as much is a significant and prolonged trend in behavior affecting asset allocation, involving pools of capital, the choice between bonds and more risky assets, and carry trade taking place in various forms.	FS2	
201510	1559	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Let me explain one particular aspect of this that probably bothers me the most, and that is the change in the behavior of people running companies and what it means today to run a nonfinancial business.	FS2	
201510	1560	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	By the way, I would say that in the sixth year of an expansion, you would normally see more excess than we are seeing, but, not to be gratuitous, I think financial regulation has helped tamp it down, except in the area of people running companies.	FS2	
201510	1561	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Increasingly, CEOs have had to become not so much operators as financial engineers.	FS2	
201510	1562	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	This is widespread.	FS2	
201510	1563	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	It is now unusual for me to talk to a CEO who is not dealing with an activist, or the fear of an activist, in their stock and who is not actively being pressured to use financial engineering, often to buy back their stock in size.	FS2	
201510	1564	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	If you ask them what they would be doing if they were a private company or without the activists, a fraction of them would actually be using their resources to do things that would yield more revenue growth.	FS2	
201510	1565	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	But their time frames are much, much shorter.	FS2	
201510	1566	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	This is because—even though there have always been activists—in the past several years, the recent emergence of very, very large pools of activist capital means that the pools of capital are dramatically bigger now, as people are willing to allocate to riskier assets.	FS2	
201510	1567	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	It’s not surprising because not only time frames are shorter, but also the tenure of CEOs in this country is much shorter.	FS2	
201510	1568	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	It’s not a coincidence.	FS2	
201510	1569	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	These pools of capital have an extremely short time horizon, to the point at which I would call them renters of the stock, not owners of the stock.	FS2	
201510	1570	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	They’re not going to be around a year from now, they may not even be around six months from now, but they will stay around long enough to get a CEO—or groups of them will get a CEO—to take an action.	FS2	
201510	1571	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	These pools of capital can’t afford to be patient because the people who bought the pool themselves have made allocations away from bonds to riskier assets.	FS2	
201510	1572	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	And they’re probably overallocated to riskier assets, so they don’t have a lot of patience either.	FS2	
201510	1573	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	So what’s the point of all of this?	FS2	
201510	1574	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	A lot of this is a byproduct—not fatal yet, maybe, but I think it’s getting to be dangerous.	FS2	
201510	1575	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Considering our dual mandate, particularly real GDP and unemployment, I think this type of behavior continuing at this scale probably cannot be helpful to our desire to foster GDP growth and lower unemployment.	FS2	
201510	1576	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	I don’t think it helps foster a flow of capital into more productive resource allocation that might actually grow revenue, create jobs, and increase productivity.	FS2	
201510	1577	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	This is something I haven’t seen before.	FS2	
201510	1578	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	I’ve seen a lot of waves of takeover artists and others that were driving mergers, and, in a way, I like that trend better.	FS2	
201510	1579	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	These activists are not owners, they are short-term traders, and I am concerned that behavior is starting to be affected in a widespread way.	FS2	
201510	1580	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	I think that, at some point, it is likely to undermine our ability to achieve our own mandate.	FS2	
201510	1581	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	That’s the comment I’d make on financial stability.	FS2	
201510	1582	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	FS2	
201510	1583	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Fischer.	FS2	
201510	1584	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	FS2	
201510	1585	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	I want to say a few words about the QS and the work that’s being done in the Office of Financial Stability.	FS2	
201510	1586	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	The QS report we received on October 20, 2015, is really extremely useful, and it’s well summarized in two charts.	FS2	
201510	1587	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	The first one is on page 3, which we’ve talked about already.	FS2	
201510	1588	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	It’s the overall evaluation of what’s going on, with the overall assessment being that risks and vulnerabilities are moderate.	FS2	
201510	1589	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	The other one is on page 32—the radar chart that is sometimes known as “the spider’s web.”	FS2	
201510	1590	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	If we look at the one on page 32, it tells a very interesting story.	FS2	
201510	1591	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	On the side that says “Financial Sector Vulnerability,” relative to what there was before the crisis, there’s almost nothing that looks dangerous until we get around to consumer credit.	FS2	
201510	1592	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	And then commercial real estate sticks out as the one area in which we’re now in worse shape, according to this, than we were in 2006.	FS2	
201510	1593	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	This is a very useful chart for concentrating the mind and telling the story in a very vivid way.	FS2	
201510	1594	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	By the way, on page 32, there are a few things for which we don’t yet have the data—as far as I can see, we don’t yet have the data for nonfinancial business—so there may be something more that is problematic.	FS2	
201510	1595	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	Now, the question is, “what are we supposed to do with this if we detect a problem area?”	FS2	
201510	1596	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	We’re likely to say, “Use macroprudential policy,” but we don’t have a whole lot of macroprudential tools.	FS2	
201510	1597	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	We have the countercyclical capital buffer, which is gradually making its way into regular examination by the Board.	FS2	
201510	1598	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	We have margin requirements, which we haven’t used for a long time.	FS2	
201510	1599	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	We have supervisory capacities, which, at least to those lenders we supervise, we can use to try to control risks.	FS2	
201510	1600	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	And we’ve got OMP, which is “open mouth policy,” which may do well in some circumstances.	FS2	
201510	1601	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	We don’t know how well this all works, and it’s something we’ll have to feel our way into.	FS2	
201510	1602	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	The other half of this—the stuff that Governor Tarullo and his colleagues in the FSOC are doing—is structural reforms, and that becomes the far more important part of what we have to do to reduce the potential costs of future crises or what might have been future crises.	FS2	
201510	1603	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	There’s a lot going on there.	FS2	
201510	1604	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	There are the very large increases in capital.	FS2	
201510	1605	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	We hope there will be improved resolution procedures soon.	FS2	
201510	1606	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	So we can feel a bit better about all of that, and we can feel that the effort that Nellie and her colleagues are putting into the surveillance is very, very useful.	FS2	
201510	1607	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	But we also have to remind ourselves that surveys like this and the work that goes on in the FSOC, which is monitoring, is essentially an intelligence exercise—it’s the exercise of an intelligence agency, which is inherently difficult.	FS2	
201510	1608	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	History tells you that however good the intelligence agencies are, they make mistakes and they miss things, which means, once again, that this is very, very important.	FS2	
201510	1609	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	Getting the structure right, getting the ability to absorb losses is probably more important, without detracting in any way from what is being done by the people doing financial stability analysis.	FS2	
201510	1610	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	The only other thing I think we have to warn ourselves about is this great concern that every time you get into a crisis, the more you look, the more you find that there are problems that you missed, and so not to believe that small sectors don’t lead occasionally to big problems, and to keep that up.	FS2	
201510	1611	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	This is very important work that’s being done.	FS2	
201510	1612	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	It needs to be accompanied by other very important work on strengthening the financial sector’s ability to deal with shocks and problems.	FS2	
201510	1613	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	FS2

201510	1615	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Kocherlakota.	FS2	
201510	1616	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	FS2	
201510	1617	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I often say in public remarks that the Federal Reserve has a much better understanding of financial system vulnerabilities and risks than it did a decade ago.	FS2	
201510	1618	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I feel safe in saying that because of the work of the QS group, and I want to thank Nellie, Andreas, and the other QS contributors from all around the System for their thorough and thoughtful measurements and analysis.	FS2	
201510	1619	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I suggested earlier today, I believe that r* will be low for many years to come.	FS2	
201510	1620	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If so, the FOMC will achieve its dual-mandate objectives only in the presence of signs of financial instability, like high and possibly volatile asset prices.	FS2	
201510	1621	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This observation suggests to me that financial-stability considerations are likely to be a central consideration at FOMC discussions for years to come.	FS2	
201510	1622	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The Committee will need to become even more systematic in its approach to these issues.	FS2	
201510	1623	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There are two key points as you think about how to become more systematic.	FS2	
201510	1624	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	First, as already noted, our statutory mandate is to promote price stability and to promote maximum employment.	FS2	
201510	1625	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I see financial stability mattering for this Committee only insofar as it affects the risks to the outlooks for inflation and unemployment.	FS2	
201510	1626	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The stance of monetary policy should not be influenced by a risk that some bondholders or collection of bondholders will lose money because of a rapid rise in term premiums.	FS2	
201510	1627	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	A relevant question for monetary policy is whether such a sudden revaluation of bonds lead to a decline in employment and prices that is not readily addressable using ex post monetary policy tools.	FS2	
201510	1628	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We need to have a way to trace the manifestations of financial instability into price and employment outcomes.	FS2	
201510	1629	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The second issue is, we need to know how changes in monetary policy would mitigate financial-stability concerns, mitigate the risks that I mentioned earlier.	FS2	
201510	1630	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Right now, I’m not sure that I even know the signs involved, let alone the magnitudes.	FS2	
201510	1631	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	For example, if we’re concerned about undue increases in house prices that could eventually lead to a housing bust, should we address the stability concerns by removing monetary accommodation to slow the rise in house prices or by adding monetary accommodation to cushion an eventual fall?	FS2	
201510	1632	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Presumably, it depends on the position you are in the cycle, but, as we know, that timing is hard to judge.	FS2	
201510	1633	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I said at the beginning, I feel the current financial stability briefing process has served the FOMC well, but I see it only as a first step.	FS2	
201510	1634	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The current approach implicitly treats financial stability as an add-on concern for the Committee’s consideration.	FS2	
201510	1635	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The Committee and its staff should aim for the financial-stability briefing to be more seamlessly integrated into Tealbook A.	FS2	
201510	1636	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Let me be somewhat more specific about what I have in mind.	FS2	
201510	1637	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Tealbook A currently focuses on how monetary policy influences the modal outlook, which is grounded in the certainty equivalence principle that emerges from quadratic objectives.	FS2	
201510	1638	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I’ve discussed both here and in various conference settings, we can incorporate financial-stability considerations by seeing them as a way in which monetary policy influences the risks to the outlook beyond just the modal outlook itself.	FS2	
201510	1639	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I would say the ultimate goal should be a Tealbook A that allows the Committee to gauge, in a systematic and quantitative way, how its monetary policy decisions will influence both the modal outlook and the risks associated with that outlook.	FS2	
201510	1640	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’m optimistic that through the collective effort of QS and monetary economists around the System, this goal could well be achievable in a couple of years.	FS2	
201510	1641	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I was at the conference that the Federal Reserve Bank of Boston hosted on macroprudential tools—a great conference—and Lars Svensson presented some interesting work that started to push in this direction, and he threw down a gauntlet.	FS2	
201510	1642	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As those of you who have heard Lars before know, he’s done that before.	FS2	
201510	1643	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	He challenged people to find big effects of monetary policy on employment and price risks through financial stability.	FS2	
201510	1644	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	He was not able to find very much along those lines in his own calculations.	FS2	
201510	1645	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	While it’s not the way the System should think about financial stability, I think it would be great for this Committee to have a more integrated approach.	FS2	
201510	1646	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you.	FS2	
201510	1647	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	FS2	
201510	1648	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	Can I just ask President Kocherlakota—this is your last meeting, so I’m not going to have this opportunity again—on that point you just made, I recall the fact that risk-management systems, particularly quantitative risk-management systems, of banks broke down because they were so bad at dealing with tail events.	FS2	
201510	1649	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I’m wondering whether there’s a similar problem here.	FS2	
201510	1650	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	The risk to financial stability—at least in the Williams–Tarullo understanding of it—is about intermediation.	FS2	
201510	1651	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	When those risks mature, it is a very big deal indeed, as we saw a few years ago, but because they tend not to go incrementally, it’s a tail-risk issue.	FS2	
201510	1652	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	And I’m wondering how the Tealbook kind of approach could be modified to incorporate tail risks in a sensible fashion like that.	FS2	
201510	1653	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think it would be useful to actually draw upon thinking beyond the people in this room to try to address this.	FS2	
201510	1654	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Jeremy Stein, I think, when he was still Governor, made reference to the idea of having an objective that would be nonquadratic in nature.	FS2	
201510	1655	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So that starts to get at moments of the distribution beyond mean and variance.	FS2	
201510	1656	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It starts to force you to think about tail events.	FS2	
201510	1657	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Tail events are hard because they don’t occur very often, but I think the way you start to force yourself to think about them is to think about not just the mean, not just the variances, as I’ve emphasized in some of my talks or that Svensson has, but also these higher moments.	FS2	
201510	1658	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’ll give you my biases upfront.	FS2	
201510	1659	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Because of the uncertainties of how monetary policy actually influences all of these financial-stability considerations, I think it’s going to be hard to build a case in which you’re going to actually tighten to trade off the deterioration of the modal outlook you might get in order to achieve the lower probability of the tail events.	FS2	
201510	1660	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But that’s the way you would start to build it—to have an objective function that was starting to bring in aspects of the probability distribution other than just the mean and the variance.	FS2	
201510	1661	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Okay.	FS2	
201510	1662	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Rosengren.	FS2	
201510	1663	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Just one follow-up to that comment.	FS2	
201510	1664	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	The Survey of Professional Forecasters does try to provide a distribution.	FS2	
201510	1665	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	I’d say it doesn’t do very well at capturing the distribution, particularly in the tail.	FS2	
201510	1666	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	So it probably means thinking a bit more in terms of simulation exercises rather than trying to do what the Survey of Professional Forecasters does because my past experience with that is, it almost never captures tail events, and very rarely did the tails seem to widen prior to actual problems occurring.	FS2	
201510	1667	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I agree with what President Rosengren is saying.	FS2	
201510	1668	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My own guess about what the Survey of Professional Forecasters does is that they have normal Gaussian errors, they generate a bunch of simulations based on that, and they look at tails based on that Gaussian distribution.	FS2	
201510	1669	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s almost like you want to use scenario analysis to try to inform yourself and then think about how monetary policy might be influencing the probability of those scenarios.	FS2	
201510	1670	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think it’s a very rich and, I hope, rewarding journey.	FS2

201510	1683	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	The most serious concerns about the outlook that were raised at the September meeting seem now to have diminished.	13	1
201510	1684	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Neither the slower economic growth in many parts of the world nor the financial market turbulence at the end of August have spilled over noticeably into the domestic economy.	13	1
201510	1685	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Private domestic final purchases grew at a robust 3.9 percent rate in the second quarter, and the Tealbook forecasts similar growth for the third quarter at a 3.8 percent rate.	13	1
201510	1686	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	The Federal Reserve Bank of Boston forecast is consistent with that outlook, with consumption expected to grow more than 3 percent in the second half of this year and residential investment continuing as a source of strength for the economy.	13	1
201510	1687	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	While the data for private domestic final purchases suggest little spillover from foreign developments to the domestic economy, strong domestic final demand has been needed to offset the greater-than-expected weakness in exports and inventories.	13	1
201510	1688	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	The effects of the appreciating dollar and continued disappointing economic growth among trading partners are largely responsible for our bank’s forecast of growth over the next six quarters only a bit faster than 2 percent.	13	1
201510	1689	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	With relatively sluggish productivity growth holding potential down, this pace of growth should gradually diminish the remaining labor market slack.	13	1
201510	1690	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	And relatively slow economic growth should also give us the flexibility to raise rates very gradually—possibly closer to current market expectations than to our last median SEP.	13	1
201510	1691	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Financial markets have recovered some of their lost ground, helped by policy easing abroad.	13	1
201510	1692	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Boston staff analysis of stock market movements since the middle of August finds little effect on the stock prices of firms with large foreign exposure.	13	1
201510	1693	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Firms with a larger share of shipments abroad generally—or, more specifically, with significant trade with China—registered cumulative stock price changes that were virtually unchanged from the middle of August to the end of September.	13	1
201510	1694	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	And since that time, stock markets have improved somewhat more.	13	1
201510	1695	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	With the recovery in stock markets since the August tumult and no notable effect on firms with the largest exposure to foreign trade, it is not surprising that the US economy has not suffered the potential collateral damage that encouraged us to defer the onset of tightening at our September meeting.	13	1
201510	1696	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	With my forecast projecting the unemployment rate to decline to 5 percent by the end of the year and continuing to decline in 2016, I am now reasonably confident that inflation will gradually move to 2 percent.	13	1
201510	1697	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	While core PCE inflation is still well below our target, recent price data do seem consistent with some firming since earlier this year.	13	1
201510	1698	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	And while we have consistently overestimated how quickly inflation will return to 2 percent, we have also consistently underestimated how quickly the unemployment rate would decline.	13	1
201510	1699	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	While U-3 is nearly at its full employment level, the broader U-6 measure is not yet back to normal.	13	1
201510	1700	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	And although U-6 has declined to 10 percent, it still needs to decline further.	13	1
201510	1701	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Work by my staff looking at U-6 and U-3 reveals that both remain elevated in some states relative to the period before the recession, consistent with our position that some slack remains in labor markets.	13	1
201510	1702	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	It is noteworthy that, in states in which U-3 has normalized, U-6 is still running above its pre-recession levels.	13	1
201510	1703	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	This may suggest that at least some of the elevated spread between U-6 and U-3 will be more persistent.	13	1
201510	1704	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	In summary, while I believe we are still somewhat above the natural rate of unemployment and I am comfortable probing a bit lower for it, especially given the low readings on inflation, I also recognize that we are already at or below the natural-rate estimates of many Committee members.	13	1
201510	1705	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Given the amount of monetary stimulus from low interest rates and our hefty balance sheet, I do not want unemployment to undershoot significantly our estimate of the natural rate of unemployment.	13	1
201510	1706	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Because we have significantly underestimated the speed at which the unemployment rate has declined over the past four to five years, we need to be cognizant of the risk that labor markets could tighten more quickly as well as more slowly than we are currently anticipating.	13	1
201510	1707	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Tomorrow, I will discuss the implications of that heightened risk.	13	1

201510	1723	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	At our last meeting, I argued that the national economic conditions were such that it would be prudent to start raising rates.	13	1
201510	1724	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	We had seen strong, sustained consumption growth and enough improvement in labor market conditions that slack seemed all but gone.	13	1
201510	1725	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	Firm monthly inflation numbers for the first half of the year made me reasonably confident that inflation would return to target over the medium run.	13	1
201510	1726	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	What’s changed since September?	13	1
201510	1727	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	The inflation reports have not altered the picture much.	13	1
201510	1728	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	Gasoline prices have dragged down top-line inflation as expected, but the core CPI was largely firm through September.	13	1
201510	1729	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	Based on the experience from January to June, I think we should continue to be confident that overall inflation will return to target if oil prices stabilize or increase at moderate rates, as predicted by the Tealbook.	13	1
201510	1730	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	The retail sales report might have been a shade lower than expected, but the ex gasoline numbers strongly suggest that consumer spending continues to be strong.	13	1
201510	1731	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	Indeed, the Tealbook is forecasting consumption growth at an annual rate of 3 percent for the second half.	13	1
201510	1732	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	As I discussed at the previous meeting, I think strong consumption growth argues for higher rates, even if we acknowledge evidence that the natural real rate might be low by historical standards.	13	1
201510	1733	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	As we discussed earlier today, estimates of the natural real rate based on semistructural approaches, such as the Laubach-Williams model and its variants, or less restrictive VAR approaches, such as those estimated by some economists in Richmond, are low but they are not negative.	13	1
201510	1734	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	In fact, the range of estimates is clustered at or just above zero.	13	1
201510	1735	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	On the other hand, the actual real rate is currently negative.	13	1
201510	1736	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	The Tealbook estimate is negative 1¼ percent using four-quarter lagged core PCE inflation, which is currently 1.3 percent, as a proxy for expected inflation, and that strikes me as at the low end of plausible estimates of expected inflation when you think about measures based on inflation compensation or surveys.	13	1
201510	1737	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	You get about negative 2 percent for the real rate if you proxy expected inflation with our inflation target, for example.	13	1
201510	1738	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	So even though the uncertainty surrounding estimates of r* is wide, the current real rate appears to be substantially below the natural rate, which suggests that we should expect the real rate to rise.	13	1
201510	1739	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	The September employment report seemed to disappoint market participants, but payroll employment growth was still substantially above the rate consistent with a stable employment-to-population ratio.	13	1
201510	1740	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	So if any slack remains in the labor market, it continues to decline.	13	1
201510	1741	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	Some of the slowdown in payroll employment certainly may represent a softening in demand, particularly in manufacturing.	13	1
201510	1742	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	However, I think we need to take seriously the notion that some of the slowdown may represent increasingly binding supply constraints.	13	1
201510	1743	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	This would be consistent with widespread anecdotal reports of increasing difficulty in finding workers and the fact that job openings, despite ticking down a bit, are still quite elevated compared with a year ago, and they’re still quite elevated relative to hiring rates, which have flattened out this year.	13	1
201510	1744	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	Indeed, slowing employment growth is just what one would expect if slack were disappearing.	13	1
201510	1745	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	One often hears the argument that if there is no longer any slack in labor markets, then we should see increasing nominal wage growth.	13	1
201510	1746	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	The presumption of a relationship between nominal wage growth and the elimination of slack makes intuitive sense, but the staff memo on compensation points out that slack is just not a major driver of nominal wage growth.	13	1
201510	1747	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	This analysis is consistent with a long line of empirical research on the cyclicality of real wages, most of which finds, at best, only weakly pro-cyclical aggregate real wages.	13	1
201510	1748	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	Furthermore, what evidence there is for procyclical wages seems predominantly attributable to observations associated with large increases in the unemployment rate.	13	1
201510	1749	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	During periods of declining unemployment or low unemployment, you don’t see much relationship at all between wages and the unemployment rate.	13	1
201510	1750	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	So in current circumstances, we shouldn’t count on wages as our canary in the coal mine.	13	1
201510	1751	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	Our September decision to wait and see was motivated to some degree by a desire for more information about the implications of global economic and financial developments for US economic growth.	13	1
201510	1752	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	On this front, the intermeeting news has made little difference for the modal outlook, it seems.	13	1
201510	1753	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	The Tealbook notes that somewhat disappointing news from emerging markets and Japan has been offset by stronger-than-expected economic growth in China and Canada.	13	1
201510	1754	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	Dollar appreciation has reversed course, US equity prices have largely erased their decline, and volatility indexes have fallen.	13	1
201510	1755	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	I think it is fair to say that, if anything, the downside concerns about the US outlook associated with global developments have subsided since September.	13	1
201510	1756	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	To summarize, consumer spending is still going strong, slack is still gone, inflation is still likely to rise, and global concerns have been allayed.	13	1
201510	1757	2015-10-28	meeting	LACKER	So I think the case to raise rates is still strong.	13	1
201510	1758	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1

201510	1778	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	As other people have said earlier today, we interpret financial stress as having receded since our last meeting.	13	1

201510	1783	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	For labor markets in the United States, I continue to see improvement, though we do have issues with some labor market indicators other than unemployment or nonfarm payroll employment.	13	1
201510	1784	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	There I would see a contest between things like claims, which are wildly low—the lowest they’ve been in many, many years—or job openings, which are very strong, and part-time for economic reasons or labor force participation, which tend to be weaker.	13	1

201510	1787	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I do take seriously the idea that job growth is going to slow from here on, and I think this is a major challenge for the Committee.	13	1
201510	1788	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	It will probably drop below 200,000 jobs per month, down to 150,000 or below, and as that slowing is occurring, we will be trying to enter into a normalization cycle.	13	1
201510	1789	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I think that’s going to be a very difficult thing for us and something that we’re going to have to face.	13	1
201510	1790	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	District and national contacts suggest that preparations are being made for a strong holiday season.	13	1
201510	1791	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I think that’s consistent with the pretty good consumption growth that we’ve seen.	13	1
201510	1792	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Regarding the risks associated with the debt ceiling, I agree with others here in expecting to see a deal soon, based on developments in the last 24 to 48 hours.	13	1
201510	1793	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Chinese economic growth came in stronger than expected during the intermeeting period.	13	1
201510	1794	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I notice that the IMF did not downgrade China’s economic outlook very meaningfully in its most recent report.	13	1
201510	1795	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I interpret these things as indications that the risk of a hard landing in China is at least diminished for now.	13	1
201510	1796	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Regarding policy, I think the risk of the Fed not moving off the zero lower bound is up dramatically since our September decision.	13	1
201510	1797	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Why do I think this?	13	1
201510	1798	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	In broad-brush terms, the staff forecast is for very slow growth, steady unemployment, and inflation low and continuing below target.	13	1
201510	1799	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	So I think that the convergence to the steady state described by Benhabib and his co- authors that we were talking about earlier today is now more likely than not for the United States, and I’m going to talk about that for just a little bit.	13	1
201510	1800	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	My main concern is that the Committee has not really thought about this as a possible outcome despite my haranguing you about it for a couple of years, and now I think we might have to take it a lot more seriously.	13	1
201510	1801	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Another thing that’s worrying me is the global convergence that’s going on.	13	1
201510	1802	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	It used to be just Japan, but now you’ve got Japan plus the euro zone.	13	1
201510	1803	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	The euro zone is going to be at zero policy rates for quite a long time, and that seems like a clear reality.	13	1
201510	1804	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	This is not a good outcome, in my view.	13	1
201510	1805	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	It’s a second-best outcome.	13	1
201510	1806	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	It would be much better to go back to the equilibrium that we know and love from the 1980s and 1990s.	13	1
201510	1807	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Most of our intuition about monetary policy comes from that equilibrium, but now I think that we’ve got a better than 50–50 chance of not being able to get back to that equilibrium.	13	1
201510	1808	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	So my main message here is that the Committee needs to face up to this likely outcome.	13	1
201510	1809	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I thought I’d just list seven of the consequences of convergence to the unintended low nominal interest rate, low inflation steady state from the Benhabib et al. analysis.	13	1
201510	1810	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	First, monetary policy becomes passive at this steady state.	13	1
201510	1811	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	What does this mean?	13	1
201510	1812	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	It means that shocks hit the economy, and monetary policy doesn’t really react.	13	1
201510	1813	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	You’re at the mercy of shocks that hit the economy.	13	1
201510	1814	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	You might think of Japan as being in this situation over the past 10 years.	13	1
201510	1815	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	They’ve had several recessions during that period.	13	1
201510	1816	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Second, promising to remain at the zero lower bound simply reinforces the equilibrium, and it is no longer counted as accommodative policy because everyone already expects that you’re going to stay at the zero lower bound.	13	1
201510	1817	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	So that’s where the steady state is.	13	1
201510	1818	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	And whether you want to promise directly that you’ll stay at the zero bound longer or you want to go through a QE route, in which the QE is interpreted as promising to stay at the zero bound longer, either way you wouldn’t have any effect if you’ve already converged to this equilibrium outcome.	13	1
201510	1819	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Third, inflation would remain below target, and you would not be able to get inflation to the target.	13	1
201510	1820	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	And just so that you’re not completely giving up hope with me, I do buy into the idea that the energy price shock is going to wear off and inflation is going to come back to target.	13	1
201510	1821	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I’m not completely buying into the Benhabib et al. steady state here.	13	1
201510	1822	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	But if we did get stuck in that steady state, inflation would just stay below target.	13	1
201510	1823	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Fourth, medium-term economic growth would still be driven by human capital and technological improvement, as it is in most of our analysis, but you wouldn’t have the accompanying stabilization policy that you would normally have.	13	1
201510	1824	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	So you’d be at the mercy of shocks that hit the economy, though long-run growth would still be driven by the same things that it’s always driven by.	13	1
201510	1825	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Fifth, the risk of an asset price bubble would be high.	13	1
201510	1826	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Real interest rates would be low.	13	1
201510	1827	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	As we were talking about earlier today, the nominal interest rate would be zero.	13	1
201510	1828	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	This would presumably put you at risk of volatile asset price movements.	13	1
201510	1829	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	One thing I sometimes emphasize here is that the policy of r equals zero, or just any policy of an interest rate peg under which the interest rate doesn’t react to shocks that come into the economy, is actually the worst policy in the New Keynesian model because it allows many volatile equilibriums to occur.	13	1
201510	1830	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	So if you think that those are realistic possibilities, you could think of that as one reason why making the policy rate equal zero forever would lead to the possibility of excessive asset price movements.	13	1
201510	1831	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Sixth, there would be no prospect of simple normalization.	13	1
201510	1832	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Talk of normalization would just dwindle to nothing.	13	1
201510	1833	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	And, seven, there would be much more contemplation of extraordinary monetary policy, such as a Shinzo Abe–type effort to get away from the zero lower bound, and all the difficulties associated with that.	13	1
201510	1834	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	So I am worried about this possibility.	13	1
201510	1835	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	I think we’ve gotten much closer to it this fall, and I’ll comment more on this possibility tomorrow.	13	1
201510	1836	2015-10-28	meeting	BULLARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1

201510	1891	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I don’t think there is anything terribly original in what I’m about to say.	13	1
201510	1892	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I think the problems with data dependence don’t manifest themselves very much in this room, because I think people tend to give their actual outlooks.	13	1
201510	1893	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	But I think these problems have been manifesting themselves in our public communications, in which a focus on one or a few pieces of data somehow implies that whether there are, for example, three good and two bad data points over the course of the next month or two is going to make a difference to policy.	13	1
201510	1894	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	As we all know, what’s going on in the economy generally doesn’t change fundamentally from month to month.	13	1
201510	1895	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	We all have, either implicitly or explicitly, our own narrative as to what is basically going on in the economy.	13	1
201510	1896	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	This narrative should be, and I think is for most of us, resistant to a few, or maybe even more than a few, incongruous data points.	13	1
201510	1897	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	To change it takes something that suggests that you have to rethink the story you’re telling yourself about the economy.	13	1
201510	1898	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	Those subtleties often don’t come through in public communication, though, particularly when coupled with the identification of a particular time when we think the data may be right for liftoff.	13	1
201510	1899	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I think it can leave people with a misimpression about how the actual decisionmaking is taking place here, and, indeed, how everybody individually is assessing the economy.	13	1
201510	1900	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I want to now explain why my basic outlook, my own narrative, hasn’t changed that much since September and explain why that outlook leaves me in the position that I’ve been in for a while now of having been externally characterized as being in the “show me” group of members of the FOMC.	13	1
201510	1901	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	In September, I think the basic components of my outlook were the following:  We had modestly above-trend economic growth, though likely with some slowing, particularly in the labor market.	13	1
201510	1902	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	There was no evidence of a pickup in inflation or, to the degree it’s relevant, nominal wage growth in the context of a globally disinflationary environment.	13	1
201510	1903	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	There were continued puzzles in various economic developments—again, particularly in the labor market—reflecting the continued difficulty of determining the degree to which we are just in an elongated reversion to pre-crisis conditions and correlations, or the degree to which secular changes may be under way that mean that those conditions and correlations may not reappear.	13	1
201510	1904	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	And, finally, there was a very high degree of uncertainty about the global economy, with potentially dramatic downside risks following the August market movements in China, and there was resulting increased upward pressure on the dollar.	13	1
201510	1905	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	So what have we seen since September?	13	1
201510	1906	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	First, there has been some softening of incoming data, particularly in industrial production, employment, and recently even in housing, which had looked to be gaining strength.	13	1
201510	1907	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	But, as several of you have commented, consumer spending has stood up fairly well during this period.	13	1
201510	1908	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	Second, I think it is probably too early to know exactly how much the labor market has slowed down or whether it has slowed down as much as the current September numbers suggest.	13	1
201510	1909	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	People have already noted that August and September job numbers are more susceptible to revision than your average monthly job numbers, so we may see some change there.	13	1
201510	1910	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	As to the external environment, possible downside risks certainly seem less dramatic than at the September meeting.	13	1
201510	1911	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	But as reflected in the actual and anticipated actions of the People’s Bank of China, the European Central Bank, and the Bank of Japan, economic prospects in those major economies are still quite uncertain, with significant remaining downside risks.	13	1
201510	1912	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	The trend of the dollar in the coming quarters really does matter—I think significantly.	13	1
201510	1913	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	It’s had a major effect on economic performance this year, and it certainly could be a disappointing source of downside risk over the next year as well.	13	1
201510	1914	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	It’s hard to say at the moment whether the upward pressure on the dollar created by the various anticipated central bank moves in the rest of the world will be offset by significantly enhanced economic growth abroad or more than transitory effects on asset prices in the United States or both.	13	1
201510	1915	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	However, in light of the relatively sober expectations regarding earnings reports, to which President Kaplan referred this morning, we can’t be sure that this will be the case.	13	1
201510	1916	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	What are the implications of these changes for my outlook and, thus, for policy?	13	1
201510	1917	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	As I said, I don’t think it changes them much.	13	1
201510	1918	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	Looking at our dual criteria of continued improvement in labor markets and reasonable assurance that inflation will return to target, I don’t see these data as having provided a reason to change that outlook very much.	13	1
201510	1919	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	With respect to labor markets, we are likely to be seeing some slowing.	13	1
201510	1920	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	Thus, while I’m still inclined to believe we can have some further improvement in labor markets without negative inflationary consequences, it seems to me, at least, even more likely that any pickup in inflation won’t get away from us.	13	1
201510	1921	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	And I think the case for a lower short-term natural rate of unemployment continues to be fairly strong.	13	1
201510	1922	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I am not going to repeat what I said last meeting—or, for that matter, in numerous earlier meetings—on the level of uncertainty that attends efforts to pin down, with any degree of precision, the natural rate or relationship of unemployment to price inflation.	13	1
201510	1923	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I will just note that the staff memo on labor compensation and the Tealbook box on Okun’s law are two further reminders of how sensitive the inferred relationships are to assumptions about unobservables.	13	1
201510	1924	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	Several of you have mentioned job openings.	13	1
201510	1925	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	The run-up to a historically high level of job openings over the past year has been hard to understand when quits and hires have not moved much in tandem, or even with a lag, in relation to openings.	13	1
201510	1926	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	Decomposition of the data showing that, for example, professional services openings have increased by 50 percent over the past year suggest the measure may be a bit misleading, a conclusion that is reinforced by the fact that using the Conference Board’s numbers for openings would leave the recent plotted points on the Beveridge curve pretty consistent with its pre-crisis shape.	13	1
201510	1927	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	But there is probably some information value in the fact that both the Labor Department’s and Conference Board’s total job openings numbers have been trending down a little bit of late, though I’m not sure how much information value there is there.	13	1
201510	1928	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	With respect to inflation, the arguments here are pretty familiar.	13	1
201510	1929	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	We have already heard a couple, and I’ll bet you that over the rest of this go-round the familiar arguments on both sides will likely be repeated.	13	1
201510	1930	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I don’t want to take direct issue with the argument that most of the current downward pressure on inflation can be attributed to declines in energy prices and increases in the dollar, but I do want to observe, again, that we are in a generally disinflationary environment.	13	1
201510	1931	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	It is also worth noting that the pattern of the past several years has been that most observers keep expecting a bigger increase in inflation than turns out to be the case.	13	1
201510	1932	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I am going to take a random example:  us, meaning both the staff and FOMC participants.	13	1
201510	1933	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	If you look at the evolution of the staff forecast in each of the past three years, the forecast for inflation toward the end of the year preceding the year forecasted was consistently 0.2 to 0.4 percentage point above where it ended up.	13	1
201510	1934	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I’m including 2015 here, assuming that the current projection in the Tealbook for 2015 ends up more or less to be the case.	13	1
201510	1935	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	Similarly, the SEP shows repeated overestimation of what core PCE inflation will be in the succeeding year.	13	1
201510	1936	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	In September 2012, the central-tendency projection of core inflation in 2013 was 1.7 to 2 percent; actual inflation in 2013 was 1.5 percent.	13	1
201510	1937	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	In September 2013, the central-tendency projection for 2014 was 1.5 to 1.7 percent; the actual was 1.4 percent.	13	1
201510	1938	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	In September 2014, the central-tendency projection for 2015 was 1.6 to 1.9 percent, and as we sit here at the end of October, we are now expecting somewhere around 1.4 percent.	13	1
201510	1939	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	In fact, if you look at those three SEPs in September preceding the beginning of the year for which the projection was made, it appears that there was only one projection of inflation among all of us during that period that turned out to get the number right, and no projections that anticipated inflation would be lower than it actually turned out to be.	13	1
201510	1940	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	As a group, we have also been wrong on real GDP growth pretty consistently in the same direction.	13	1
201510	1941	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	As President Rosengren pointed out, we have been wrong on employment pretty consistently as well.	13	1
201510	1942	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	There is quite likely some correlation in GDP and inflation, but something else is going on with respect to unemployment.	13	1
201510	1943	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	That pattern suggests that there may be some underlying fundamentals affecting inflation, and possibly economic growth, in a more persistent way, and that we mistake the transitory nature of specific depressing factors for below-target inflation itself being transitory.	13	1
201510	1944	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	Now, just because that has been the case for the past three years doesn’t mean it’s going to be the case indefinitely in the future.	13	1
201510	1945	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	But when I put the difficulties of using past correlations together with our systematic overestimation of what inflation will be as well as with the number of questions that many of us are asking inside and outside this room about possible secular changes in the economy, it does put me in a position of wanting to rely on data that provide more direct evidence of meeting the inflation target than what I would term “inferential evidence,” or evidence from which inferences can be drawn.	13	1
201510	1946	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	So what I will be looking for over the course of the next couple of months and beyond are data points that make an argument for changing that outlook.	13	1
201510	1947	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	And I can certainly imagine what they may be.	13	1
201510	1948	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I can imagine them in the next couple of months, and I can imagine them taking a little bit longer.	13	1
201510	1949	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	I’ll end by saying that while I do think we should resist the tendency to be data dependent in a talismanic fashion, we certainly don’t want to be data insensitive either.	13	1
201510	1950	2015-10-28	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1

201510	2004	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	There has been much discussion about the lack of acceleration in wages and what it might mean, and the memo on wages and slack did an excellent job of reviewing what we can and cannot glean from wage data.	13	1

201510	2008	2015-10-28	meeting	POWELL	If, by contrast, actual businesses were looking at shorter-term trends, which I suspect might be the case, then one could argue that wage growth is relatively high, as productivity has for a number of years been well below the 1½ percent trend used by the model.	13	1

201510	2054	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	The second issue relates to wage growth.	13	1
201510	2055	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	When talking about prices, I keep being bothered about the emphasis that is placed on wage growth and why it hasn’t risen.	13	1
201510	2056	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	I read with interest the memo that was sent to us by Deb Lindner, John Roberts, and Bill Wascher on compensation and labor market slack.	13	1
201510	2057	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	I thought the memo was remarkably clear, and I was particularly struck by their specification of the wage equation, which showed wage growth depending, inter alia, on a weighted moving average of structural productivity growth.	13	1
201510	2058	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	With structural productivity growth having been so weak in recent years, it is probably not surprising that we are seeing disappointing real wage growth.	13	1
201510	2059	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	That is, on the demand side, it’s hard to see why, when productivity is barely rising, real wages would be expected to be rising at any significant rate.	13	1
201510	2060	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	Having said that, there may nonetheless be more wage gains occurring in the economy than we are seeing in our favorite wage measures.	13	1
201510	2061	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	The Lindner, Roberts, and Wascher memo showed in its figure 6 that some measures of wage pressures have been rising more robustly than the employment cost index and average hourly earnings.	13	1
201510	2062	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	And in March, we had an alternative- view Tealbook box in which Jeremy Nalewaik showed that these alternative measures do a pretty good job of predicting current-year growth of the wages and salaries component of compensation per hour.	13	1
201510	2063	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	We are also aware that the actual data on compensation per hour are often subject to large revisions of their initial estimates.	13	1
201510	2064	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	We have also seen the forecast, which I have mentioned several times today—and I apologize if I keep coming back to it—that the staff is predicting labor productivity growth, which includes the effect of capital deepening, will return to 1½ percent per annum next year.	13	1
201510	2065	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	And that would indicate some moving up of wages and perhaps some pressure on prices.	13	1
201510	2066	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	So I continue to expect that we will see inflation moving toward our target before too long.	13	1
201510	2067	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	I still believe we are meeting the criteria that the labor market continues to strengthen.	13	1
201510	2068	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	Subject to discussions on how much it takes to keep it at the same level of strength as it is now, we will be able to see that in the data early next month and then early in December.	13	1
201510	2069	2015-10-28	meeting	FISCHER	I do believe that we are going to see inflation rising toward 2 percent in the medium run, but we will come back to several of these issues tomorrow.	13	1

201510	2086	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The District unemployment rate continued to decline in September to 4.7 percent, which is below the national rate of 5.1.	13	1
201510	2087	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Ohio’s unemployment rate ticked down to 4.5 percent.	13	1
201510	2088	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	This is below the Cleveland Federal Reserve staff’s 4.8 percent estimate of the state’s longer-run normal unemployment rate, which is based on a model of labor market flows.	13	1
201510	2089	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Reports of upward wage pressure were largely concentrated in specialized occupations like construction and IT.	13	1
201510	2090	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Construction contacts reported that the primary downside risk they face is a shortage of labor, not higher interest rates.	13	1
201510	2091	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Prices of finished goods and inputs were generally steady except for lower prices for products related to oil, such as steel and plastic.	13	1
201510	2092	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Turning to the national economy, last December the Committee shifted its forward guidance to emphasize the data dependence of its policy decisions.	13	1
201510	2093	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Forward guidance has gone through several formulations as the economy has emerged from the crisis and recession and as we’re moving toward more normal conditions.	13	1
201510	2094	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	With the end of the asset purchase program, data dependence signaled we were getting back to a more normal policy setting as well.	13	1
201510	2095	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	In my view, one of the unintended consequences of data dependence is that it has contributed to the natural inclination of market participants and others to focus on short-run developments in the economy rather than on the medium-run outlook, which is the more appropriate time horizon for monetary policy decisions.	13	1
201510	2096	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Similarly, I have some concerns that the public’s expectations about what constitutes a healthy economy are not necessarily in sync with reality.	13	1
201510	2097	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	I think our communications should play a role in helping the public interpret the monthly data releases.	13	1
201510	2098	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	We can put them into context and create a narrative that provides a proper gauge with which to evaluate economic developments.	13	1
201510	2099	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The incoming data since our last meeting have been mixed.	13	1
201510	2100	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Economic growth slowed in the third quarter, which is perhaps not unexpected after the second quarter’s strong 3.9 percent pace.	13	1
201510	2101	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The question is whether this slowdown is pointing to a loss of momentum that would change the medium-run outlook.	13	1
201510	2102	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	My current read on that question is similar to the Tealbook’s.	13	1
201510	2103	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	I haven’t changed my medium-run outlook.	13	1
201510	2104	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	After a temporary slowdown, I expect economic growth to pick back up to an above-trend pace in 2016.	13	1
201510	2105	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The consumer sector remains strong.	13	1
201510	2106	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Investment has strengthened, and labor markets continue to improve.	13	1
201510	2107	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The stronger dollar and slower economic growth abroad have restrained growth in manufacturing, and the decline in oil prices continues to weaken investment in the energy sector.	13	1
201510	2108	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	However, we’ve recently seen some stability in both the dollar and oil prices.	13	1
201510	2109	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The volatility seen in financial markets at the time of our last meeting, which largely reflected a reassessment of growth abroad, has largely died down.	13	1
201510	2110	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	These are welcome developments, but it’s important to acknowledge that slowing in the global economy does remain a downside risk to the forecast.	13	1
201510	2111	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The increase in nonfarm payrolls in September was 142,000, and the August increase was revised down to 136,000, which is slower than the average pace seen earlier this year.	13	1
201510	2112	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The unemployment rate held steady at 5.1 percent.	13	1
201510	2113	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	As we often say, it’s important not to read too much into one month’s employment report, in view of the month-to-month variation in these readings.	13	1
201510	2114	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	But even if these numbers do not get revised up, as they have in recent years, we need to put them in their context.	13	1
201510	2115	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	With the economy at or nearly at full employment, we should expect the pace of job growth and declines in the unemployment rate to slow.	13	1
201510	2116	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	How we characterize developments is going to be very important for setting reasonable expectations regarding progress in the labor market, which is an important part of the criteria we’ve set for liftoff.	13	1
201510	2117	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	In my view, labor markets continued to improve over the intermeeting period.	13	1
201510	2118	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	First, the pace of job growth we’ve seen over the past two months, though slower than what we saw earlier this year, is consistent with continued declines in unemployment.	13	1
201510	2119	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Across different models and assumptions about labor force participation, estimates suggest that monthly payroll growth in the 75,000 to 120,000 range is sufficient to put downward pressure on the unemployment rate.	13	1
201510	2120	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	So the September increase in payrolls is well above the range of steady-state growth.	13	1
201510	2121	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Last month we also saw declines in many of the broader estimates of underemployment, such as the long- term unemployment rate and the U-6 measure.	13	1
201510	2122	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Taken together, I think September’s employment report is consistent with further improvement in the labor market.	13	1
201510	2123	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	I say this even though the Board staff’s labor market condition index, which summarizes developments across a wide range of labor market statistics, was unchanged in September.	13	1
201510	2124	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	We’re lucky enough to have Bruce Fallick on the staff at the Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland.	13	1
201510	2125	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Bruce is one of the authors of the research behind the LMCI.	13	1
201510	2126	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	One of the things he pointed out is that the index makes use of estimated statistical trends for each included data series.	13	1
201510	2127	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Since the Great Recession, the trends underlying the LMCI have been excessively cyclical, which means that the gaps have been insufficiently cyclical.	13	1
201510	2128	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The implied trend of private payroll growth used in constructing the LMCI has moved higher, and it’s now considerably higher than the private payroll growth that would be consistent with the 75,000 to 120,000 range for trend total payroll growth I just mentioned.	13	1
201510	2129	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	So the LMCI interpreted September’s private payroll gain of 118,000 as being well below trend, and this pulled down the index substantially.	13	1
201510	2130	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The LMCI is likely then underestimating the improvement in labor markets during the expansion, while it understated the deterioration of labor markets during the recession.	13	1
201510	2131	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	What this means is that if the LMCI is overestimating trend private payroll growth by even a small amount, which seems likely, then the index reading would have been above zero in September, consistent with the improvement we saw in a number of labor market indicators, including the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City’s labor market momentum index.	13	1
201510	2132	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Turning to inflation, incoming data are consistent with the inflation dynamics that the Committee has been expecting.	13	1
201510	2133	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	As oil prices and the value of the dollar have begun to stabilize, the downward pressure on inflation from earlier changes has started to abate.	13	1
201510	2134	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Headline inflation remains well below our 2 percent target, but recent readings on underlying inflation, including the core, trimmed mean, and median CPI measures, have moved up.	13	1
201510	2135	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Inflation, as measured by the year-over-year change in the Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland’s median CPI measure, rose to 2½ percent in September.	13	1
201510	2136	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	There’s considerable uncertainty around any inflation forecast, but analysis by Cleveland Reserve Bank staff and others suggests that core measures of inflation can improve forecasts of headline inflation, at least over some time horizons.	13	1
201510	2137	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	In some cases, the improvement is statistically significant.	13	1
201510	2138	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Thus, I find firming in the core measures to be a factor supporting the modal forecast that inflation will gradually move toward 2 percent over the medium run.	13	1
201510	2139	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The other important factor supporting the forecast is stable longer-term inflation expectations.	13	1
201510	2140	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The Tealbook’s analysis suggests we should be cautious in inferring much signal from inflation expectations from the recent downward movement in market-based measures of inflation compensation.	13	1
201510	2141	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The survey-based measures have been broadly stable.	13	1
201510	2142	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland’s measure of 10-year inflation expectations is higher than it was at the beginning of the year.	13	1
201510	2143	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	It did move down slightly in October, but that reflected a change in near-term inflation.	13	1
201510	2144	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	The five-year, five-year-forward measure was essentially unchanged in October at a bit less than 2 percent.	13	1
201510	2145	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	With inflation expectations anchored, the effects on inflation of past declines in energy prices and dollar appreciation beginning to dissipate, and the economy expected to resume growing at an above-trend pace after a weak third quarter, I think we can be reasonably confident that inflation will move gradually back to our target over the next few years.	13	1
201510	2146	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	I do not want to imply that there are no risks associated with the forecast, or that there are no developments that would shake confidence in the outlook.	13	1
201510	2147	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	For example, a significant slowdown in consumer spending or in payroll growth would necessitate a reassessment.	13	1
201510	2148	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	But if economic developments continue along the lines we’ve seen in recent months, I would view them as indicating that the conditions we’ve set for liftoff have been met, and I think a prudent course of action would be to follow through in December.	13	1
201510	2149	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Thereafter, should either downside risks or upside risks actually manifest themselves, the Committee will be in a position to adjust the pace of further policy actions around a path that is already anticipated to be a gradual one.	13	1
201510	2150	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	I view this course as balancing the various risks, while maintaining the credibility of our earlier communications.	13	1
201510	2151	2015-10-28	meeting	MESTER	Of course, we need to ensure that the communications coming out of this meeting give us the opportunity to implement this course, and we’ll talk about that tomorrow.	13	1

201510	2154	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Kaplan.	13	1
201510	2155	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201510	2156	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	I’m going to start, as I did in the September meeting, with the outlook for the energy sector.	13	1
201510	2157	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	You may remember that we said in the last FOMC meeting that the daily global supply of oil exceeded demand by approximately 2 million barrels a day.	13	1
201510	2158	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	That has improved slightly since then; our best estimate is that it is now about 1.8 million barrels a day—mainly because of a reduction in drilling in the United States, which I’ll talk more about, and a slight firming of demand estimates for China.	13	1
201510	2159	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	In addition to this supply and demand, though, we estimate that the current OECD inventory of oil stands at about 3 billion barrels.	13	1
201510	2160	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	In our view, that is an excess inventory of approximately 300 million barrels.	13	1
201510	2161	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	For the oil market to get into some degree of overall balance, daily supply and demand first needs to get to some level of balance, and we still have a ways to go.	13	1
201510	2162	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Then there needs to be sufficient excess demand to begin to work off this high level of inventory.	13	1
201510	2163	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	In our view, we’re not likely to get to the first part of this process, the balance of daily supply and demand, until late 2016 or early 2017.	13	1
201510	2164	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	This helps explain why there has been so much volatility—which I’ll comment on in a moment—in the price of oil.	13	1
201510	2165	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	There are several factors we’re going to be closely watching.	13	1
201510	2166	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	One is, obviously, ongoing reductions in capital expenditures.	13	1
201510	2167	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Rig counts just since the September FOMC meeting have decreased 9 percent.	13	1
201510	2168	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	We will continue to closely watch expected demand from US consumers as well as expected demand from emerging market countries, particularly China.	13	1
201510	2169	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	We will be watching political and military events in the Middle East, which threaten disruption.	13	1
201510	2170	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	This is why Russia’s intervention in Syria several weeks ago appeared to have such an effect on oil prices, because it raised fears of disruption.	13	1
201510	2171	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Ironically, these temporary upward spikes in oil prices may, in fact, ultimately slow the process of getting into supply–demand balance because it might cause producers to pause or sell into the futures market instead of further reducing their capital expenditures.	13	1
201510	2172	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	We continue to believe that Iran and Libya will come back online, which we now believe will add approximately 3¾ million barrels per day to world oil production.	13	1
201510	2173	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	With all this said, this is still a process in which several possible endings could unfold, and we should expect that there will be significant swings up and down in the price of oil because participants are struggling to get a grip on the speed of this balancing process.	13	1
201510	2174	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	In the meantime, in the United States, at least, we expect to see more bankruptcies, restructurings, and mergers because, as we’ve said before, the pain of these reductions in supply is going to be felt at its worst in the United States because the United States, particularly in Texas and, to some extent, North Dakota—and, to some extent, in Canada as well—has the highest concentration of marginal production—that is, shale.	13	1
201510	2175	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	With all of that background, I’ll turn back to the 11th District.	13	1
201510	2176	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	This oil situation has continued to create negative spillovers, as we reported last time, in our District.	13	1
201510	2177	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	For starters, the new orders component of our manufacturing survey was negative 4.6 percent in September and negative 7.6 percent in October versus an August reading of negative 12.5 percent.	13	1
201510	2178	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Meanwhile, the service sector in Texas continues to grow and add jobs, with the health-care, leisure, and hospitality sectors driving much of the increase.	13	1
201510	2179	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Overall employment in the District has, to us, been surprisingly resilient given the diversified nature of the Texas economy to date.	13	1
201510	2180	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	In addition, we’ve had a significant expansion of the petrochemical industry in the Gulf Coast because of the low price of natural gas, which is a key input into petrochemicals.	13	1
201510	2181	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	So we’ve got a boom in that industry going on in Texas at the same time the oil industry itself is contracting.	13	1
201510	2182	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	We had, through July, an annualized rate of job growth in Texas of 1.4 percent.	13	1
201510	2183	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	That dropped to still-positive job growth in August and September of 0.7 percent.	13	1
201510	2184	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	We are expecting 0.8 percent growth for the remainder of the year.	13	1
201510	2185	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	And when all is said and done, our judgment is that job growth for 2015 in Texas will still be a positive 1.2 percent, compared with 3.6 percent growth in 2014.	13	1
201510	2186	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Despite weakened job growth this year, the unemployment rate in Texas has held basically steady around 4.1 or 4.2 percent for the past eight months.	13	1
201510	2187	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	This low rate of unemployment, by the way, is even lower in cities like Dallas, Austin, and San Antonio—that is, in cities not predominantly reliant on oil.	13	1

201510	2190	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	In terms of wage pressure, both the manufacturing and service-sector surveys suggest upward wage pressures since June, and the outlook for wage growth among respondents continues to be strongly positive for the next six months.	13	1
201510	2191	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	In terms of commercial real estate, we see, not surprisingly, higher vacancy rates in cities like Houston, but we continue to see strong commercial real estate in other cities, like Dallas–Fort Worth and San Antonio.	13	1

201510	2196	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	But in the service sector, our selling price index still went up 3 percent in September and 3½ percent in October.	13	1
201510	2197	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	So we have a number of crosscurrents in the District.	13	1
201510	2198	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	On the national economy, our views are not materially different than they were in September.	13	1
201510	2199	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	We still expect to see the unemployment rate reach its longer-run sustainable rate of 5 percent by the end of this year and to fall below that level thereafter.	13	1
201510	2200	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	The August reading for the Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas trimmed mean PCE inflation rate was 1.67 percent year over year.	13	1
201510	2201	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	If you take six months over six months, the rate was 1.97 percent, or almost 2 percent for the past six months.	13	1
201510	2202	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Our 2016 forecast for inflation is 1.8 percent, and we continue to expect inflation to reach our 2 percent objective by the end of 2017.	13	1
201510	2203	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	Lastly, as we said before, we continue to believe economic events in non-US countries, particularly Japan, China, Turkey, and Brazil, have the potential to negatively affect the United States for an extended period of time.	13	1
201510	2204	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	In particular, we believe issues such as the level of debt to GDP in these countries; the need, particularly in China, to restructure state-owned enterprises; efforts, particularly in China, to shift from an economy driven by investments and net exports to one that is consumer oriented; severe demographic challenges in several countries, particularly China; and other secular challenges will create headwinds for US real GDP growth as well as inflation for an extended period of time.	13	1
201510	2205	2015-10-28	meeting	KAPLAN	The one thing that makes us feel somewhat better, though, is that, as Governor Powell mentioned, stock markets outside the United States, particularly in China, look, at least to us, like they have adjusted to a great degree to this new reality.	13	1
