201510	2209	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201510	2210	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	With the exception of the state of Colorado, the pace of economic activity in the 10th District has slowed compared with the nation.	13	1
201510	2211	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	While unemployment rates remain low in most District states, employment growth is flat or down over the past six months due to weakness in energy and agriculture, which appears to now be spilling over into other sectors.	13	1
201510	2212	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	In the energy sector, our District energy survey paints a picture of continued headwinds.	13	1
201510	2213	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	With the breakeven price of oil at around $60, District firms are not expecting a return to profitability for some time.	13	1
201510	2214	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	As a result, capital spending plans are being downgraded and firms continue to shed workers, though anecdotal evidence suggests that some displaced workers are finding jobs in sectors such as aerospace.	13	1
201510	2215	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Some District energy companies have faced credit downgrades, and one company recently filed for bankruptcy in an attempt to shed around $3 billion in debt from its balance sheet.	13	1
201510	2216	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Conditions in the District’s agriculture sector also remain soft.	13	1
201510	2217	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Cattle prices, which had been holding up, have declined, and prices for commodities such as wheat and corn remain low.	13	1
201510	2218	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Overall, weakening farm income has led to increased borrowing by farmers and less capital spending.	13	1
201510	2219	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Finally, weak demand for exports has weighed on manufacturing activity, which, according to our District manufacturing survey, has declined for eight straight months.	13	1
201510	2220	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	These business contacts point to international conditions as a primary factor weighing on demand for their products.	13	1
201510	2221	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	For the national economy, I expect economic growth to average around 2 percent in the second half of 2015 before moving up to around 2¾ percent next year.	13	1
201510	2222	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	The drag due to the behavior of net exports is likely to continue to weigh on headline growth.	13	1
201510	2223	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Otherwise, growth in private domestic demand has been running at more than 3½ percent for more than a year, a pace that looks to have continued and possibly strengthened in the third quarter.	13	1
201510	2224	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Given demographic and productivity trends, I view this pace of domestic growth as quite robust.	13	1
201510	2225	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Consumer spending, of course, has been a driving factor.	13	1
201510	2226	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Notwithstanding the sluggishness of core retail sales in recent months, they increased at a solid pace in the third quarter.	13	1
201510	2227	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Vehicle sales and consumer confidence remain at high levels.	13	1
201510	2228	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	The momentum in consumer spending appears broad based, and growth in real PCE has steadily picked up over the past few years.	13	1
201510	2229	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	For example, the year-over-year growth in consumer spending was about 1.3 percent at the end of 2012.	13	1
201510	2230	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Now it’s running at 3.3 percent.	13	1
201510	2231	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	In contrast, net exports have weighed on the outlook, and I see them subtracting about ¾ percentage point from GDP growth this year, reflecting the stronger dollar and soft foreign economic growth.	13	1
201510	2232	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Because this forecast has taken developments in recent months in financial and international markets on board, I view the risk from abroad as currently balanced.	13	1
201510	2233	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Along these lines, domestic and foreign financial markets have largely stabilized since our previous meeting.	13	1
201510	2234	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	The recent deceleration of payroll employment growth bears watching in coming months, but we have seen such bumps before, most recently this March.	13	1
201510	2235	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	So it’s hard to judge whether this deceleration stems from the start of a more general slump in economic activity or slowing momentum in the pace of employment gains.	13	1
201510	2236	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Given the momentum we’ve experienced over the past two years, I do expect some moderation in the monthly pace of payroll growth over the next year to below 200,000 because of demographic factors and the fact that the economy is likely close to full employment.	13	1
201510	2237	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Even if the slower pace of the past two months continues in the next two employment reports, such a pace would continue to comfortably exceed the minimum growth necessary to continue reducing labor market slack.	13	1
201510	2238	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Of course, the slowing in employment growth would be more concerning if measures of unemployment were rising.	13	1
201510	2239	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	That said, initial claims are currently very low, at levels last reached in 1973, and the U-6 broad unemployment rate continues to make steady downward progress.	13	1
201510	2240	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	While most measures of labor market slack have shown significant improvement over the past two years and suggest that only a modest amount remains, some have pointed to lingering concerns that persistently low levels of nominal wage growth are indicative of higher levels of slack.	13	1
201510	2241	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Recent research by my staff aligns with the findings in the Board staff memo showing that current measures of wage growth are in line with readings from our main labor market indicators.	13	1
201510	2242	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Using the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City’s labor market index as a broad measure of labor market conditions, the analysis found that a model of real wage growth based on this index and labor productivity predicts real wage growth consistent with actual real nominal wage growth.	13	1
201510	2243	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	This leads me to believe that the current low level of real wage growth is related more to weak productivity growth and not necessarily to hidden slack.	13	1
201510	2244	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	Finally, turning to inflation, the foreign exchange value of the dollar and energy price movements have been primary factors affecting my outlook.	13	1
201510	2245	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	While core inflation remains somewhat low, it has been stable in 2015.	13	1
201510	2246	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	The 12-month change in the core PCE index has stayed at 1.3 percent since January, even amid further appreciation of the dollar and additional declines in energy prices.	13	1
201510	2247	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	This may suggest that diminishing slack has been compensating for downward pressure arising from these temporary factors.	13	1
201510	2248	2015-10-28	meeting	GEORGE	If so, as the effects of these temporary factors fade, it would seem reasonable to expect that both headline and core inflation will begin to move toward our 2 percent goal.	13	1

201510	2251	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Evans.	13	1
201510	2252	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201510	2253	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	The positive reports I received this round were outnumbered by the more pessimistic comments.	13	1
201510	2254	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	On the plus side, automakers continue to be quite optimistic about their ability to sustain high sales rates into 2016.	13	1
201510	2255	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Of course, low auto loan rates have helped sustain the 17.2 million unit sales pace we’ve seen so far this year.	13	1
201510	2256	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	In addition, reports on bank lending in the Seventh District were quite strong.	13	1
201510	2257	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	That said, many of my contacts were somewhat more pessimistic than they were in September.	13	1
201510	2258	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	The gloom was most pronounced among manufacturers who have a large international business exposure.	13	1
201510	2259	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	They’ve been downbeat for a while, but things took a turn for the worse this round.	13	1
201510	2260	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Steelmakers were particularly dour, as they are looking at global excess capacity and prices that are down 30 percent or so from a year ago.	13	1
201510	2261	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	They see no market-based relief in sight and have filed legal challenges to help counter alleged dumping.	13	1
201510	2262	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Heavy- equipment manufacturers were nearly as pessimistic as steelmakers, as they continue to report weak demand and additional layoffs.	13	1
201510	2263	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	However, not all of the new concerns came from the international front.	13	1
201510	2264	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Our Beige Book survey showed sharp cutbacks in hiring and capital spending plans not just among manufacturers, but also by firms in the service sectors.	13	1
201510	2265	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Low crop prices are weighing on rural economies, and, notably, we heard a good deal of talk from our contacts in capital markets regarding tighter credit conditions in the United States.	13	1
201510	2266	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	These reports go beyond financing for the energy sector or commodity-dependent businesses.	13	1
201510	2267	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	We also heard of wider bond spreads for domestic health-care and technology firms, and even Ford noted that spreads had increased on their commercial paper.	13	1
201510	2268	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	In culling through these reports, it seems difficult to separate the contributions from the strong dollar, weak foreign growth emanating from China and other emerging market economies, and tighter domestic financial conditions.	13	1
201510	2269	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	But on top of these, there also seems to have been a noticeable pullback in risk appetite across a range of industries.	13	1
201510	2270	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	I suppose an executive summary of this was my conversation with a Fortune 100 CEO who asked me if I was hearing anything positive, because virtually none of his business roundtable counterparts was optimistic.	13	1
201510	2271	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	The front page story in the Wall Street Journal on Monday captured this sentiment very well, too.	13	1
201510	2272	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	For today, I’m filing all of these concerns as important risks to monitor, but not yet as factors that we ought to build into a weaker baseline outlook.	13	1
201510	2273	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	So like the Tealbook, our forecast for economic activity hasn’t changed much from September.	13	1
201510	2274	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	For now, we too continue to see economic growth running moderately above trend over the next couple of years.	13	1
201510	2275	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	The incoming data have not raised my confidence that inflation is poised to return to target over the medium term.	13	1
201510	2276	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Our full suite of models still forecasts 2018 inflation in the 1¼ to 1¾ percent range.	13	1
201510	2277	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	The Tealbook box that I referred to on I(1) inflation in term structure models got me thinking:  One way or another, I suppose, all of our FOMC forecasts have inflation getting back to our 2 percent objective at some point in time.	13	1
201510	2278	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	The question is, how fast will we get there?	13	1
201510	2279	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	More specifically, do we have models that forecast quick convergence to 2 percent yet still match the degree of persistence observed in the actual inflation data?	13	1
201510	2280	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	I’m skeptical that we do.	13	1
201510	2281	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	At the Federal Reserve Bank of Chicago, we have two structural models that are capable of generating persistence in the data if the data call for it, and these both see slow convergence of inflation back to our 2 percent objective.	13	1
201510	2282	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	One is our term structure model, which will forecast persistently low inflation whenever the stationary but highly persistent level factor from the yield curve data is low.	13	1
201510	2283	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	That is an important reason why today this model is predicting that inflation in 2018 will only be back up to about 1½ percent.	13	1
201510	2284	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	The other is the Federal Reserve Bank of Chicago DSGE model.	13	1
201510	2285	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	It can generate slow convergence back to target because real rigidities mean certain shocks will have persistent disinflationary effects.	13	1
201510	2286	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	The model identifies that such shocks have been important in explaining the low economic growth and low inflation of the past several years.	13	1
201510	2287	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	As a result, our DSGE model is also projecting inflation at just 1½ percent in 2018.	13	1
201510	2288	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	I recognize that there are many different models of inflation, that prescient inflation forecasting is difficult to come by, and that our uncertainty measures are pretty large in this arena.	13	1
201510	2289	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Many statistical models have stationarity and rapid mean reversion to 2 percent inflation essentially baked in the cake, but these models also fail to match the persistence actually observed in the inflation data.	13	1
201510	2290	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	In contrast, models with unit-root I(1) inflation processes or stochastic volatility or both can generate much more persistent deviations from 2 percent, as can models with strong real rigidities and persistent stationary factors, such as our Chicago Fed DSGE and term structure models.	13	1
201510	2291	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	I put more weight on these latter models.	13	1
201510	2292	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	So my bottom line is that I have difficulty feeling confident that inflation is headed back to 2 percent any time soon.	13	1
201510	2293	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Also, I have to believe an important ingredient in the President Lacker/President Bullard/Benhabib/Schmitt-Grohe/Uribe low inflation equilibrium is that the public is expecting inflation to be below our 2 percent objective.	13	1
201510	2294	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	So, again, I think that committing in public that we are focused on getting inflation symmetrically up to 2 percent is an important feature of knocking out that low equilibrium.	13	1
201510	2295	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	One other thing that occurred to me in looking around the table as President Bullard was making his comments is that I’m not sure how many people understand that model analysis.	13	1
201510	2296	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Frankly, when I hear comments in my Bank that I don’t understand and that I think might be bad, I try to make sure everybody tries to figure it out or I get some advice.	13	1
201510	2297	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	I think that it is probably incumbent on us to have somebody, perhaps from the Federal Reserve Banks of Richmond and St Louis, put together a briefing memo so we all understand this important feature, if it is important.	13	1
201510	2298	2015-10-28	meeting	EVANS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1

201510	2300	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Williams.	13	1
201510	2301	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201510	2302	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	I suggest that President Evans come to Tomorrowland in the 12th District and meet with my directors, because you mostly were seeing the people who were pessimistic, and as one of my advisory council members said, “In La-La Land, no one is pessimistic.”	13	1
201510	2303	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	[Laughter]	13	1
201510	2304	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	At the previous meeting, I argued that the economy was finally in good enough shape to begin monetary policy normalization, and since then the data received have only reinforced that view.	13	1
201510	2305	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Importantly, the key global economic and financial concerns that stayed our hand in September have receded.	13	1
201510	2306	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Financial market volatility has moderated as data from China and other emerging markets have come in at or above expectations, and fears of a hard landing have diminished.	13	1
201510	2307	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	As noted in the QS report, the US financial system weathered this global turbulence without any systemic strains, and, furthermore, US equity markets have retraced more than half of their late summer selloff.	13	1
201510	2308	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Altogether, these intermeeting improvements resulted in more favorable readings in a variety of broad indexes of financial conditions.	13	1
201510	2309	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	On balance, US macroeconomic data since our previous meeting have also been in line with my expectations.	13	1
201510	2310	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	The monthly data on the crucial drivers of final demand, such as consumer spending and business investment, have continued to show solid gains.	13	1
201510	2311	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Despite these gains, third-quarter real GDP growth will likely be held down by the inventory correction.	13	1
201510	2312	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Still, real GDP remains on track to grow at 2 percent this year, and that’s the same average pace of growth we’ve seen over the past five years, which was fast enough to bring the unemployment rate down from 10 percent to close to 5 percent.	13	1
201510	2313	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	I view the risks to the solid economic growth forecast as well balanced.	13	1
201510	2314	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	As I’ve already mentioned, the downside tail risks from abroad have diminished in two ways.	13	1
201510	2315	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	First, the probability of an adverse fallout and contagion among emerging markets has come down.	13	1
201510	2316	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Second, some systemic resilience to such shocks has been demonstrated for the next episode of volatility.	13	1
201510	2317	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	On the upside, domestic growth could certainly surpass my expectations.	13	1
201510	2318	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	For example, residential construction continues to perk up.	13	1
201510	2319	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Both homebuilder sentiment and household formation have jumped to new highs for this expansion, and that should support increased building.	13	1
201510	2320	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Turning to the labor data, the recent jobs report did not change my view that the unemployment rate will fall to 4.7 percent by the middle of next year.	13	1
201510	2321	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Under current labor force and population dynamics, any monthly job growth above 100,000 will tend to reduce labor market slack.	13	1
201510	2322	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Indeed, employment grew by less than 140,000 jobs per month on average in August and September, and that pace was enough to lower U-3, U-4, and U-5 by 0.2 percentage point each and U-6 by 0.4 percentage point, continuing their ongoing downward trends.	13	1
201510	2323	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Given the strength of the economy and the lagged effects of any near-term policy tightening, I’m confident that the economy will overshoot full employment next year.	13	1
201510	2324	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Unfortunately, I have become much more pessimistic that such overshooting will boost labor force participation.	13	1
201510	2325	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	My staff has reexamined the prospects for a cyclical resurgence in participation, and the data are not showing the rebound that was hoped for.	13	1
201510	2326	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	In particular, it is disappointing how few have entered the labor force during this recovery despite the substantial increase in employment.	13	1
201510	2327	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	For example, consider the “marginally attached.”	13	1
201510	2328	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	These are nonparticipants who report that they’re willing and available to work, who have searched for work within the past year, but who aren’t currently looking for a job.	13	1
201510	2329	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	This marginally attached group contains the candidates most likely to reenter the labor market.	13	1
201510	2330	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	However, as the unemployment rate dropped over the past five years, the reentry rates for the marginally attached have actually come down.	13	1
201510	2331	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	A similar pattern emerges for other groups of nonparticipants.	13	1
201510	2332	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	These results are consistent with evidence recently highlighted by Alan Krueger that labor force entry rates are not cyclical.	13	1
201510	2333	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	All in all, this suggests that overshooting full employment by running a high-pressure economy will not bring a large number of those out of the labor force back into it at a faster pace.	13	1
201510	2334	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	It also suggests that nonparticipants are not really an indicator of a significant degree of more slack.	13	1
201510	2335	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	I would like to make one point on that.	13	1
201510	2336	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	I find the Board staff’s calculation of the potential worker rate to be very useful.	13	1
201510	2337	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	It appears, I think, in the Monday morning Board briefing.	13	1
201510	2338	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Basically, it’s the unemployed plus the nonparticipants who say they want a job, which is a pretty broad definition of unemployed.	13	1
201510	2339	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	It’s broader than U-5, so you could think of it as “U-5 extended,” or “U-5 plus.”	13	1
201510	2340	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	That rate has come down significantly—more than 1 percentage point in the past 12 months.	13	1
201510	2341	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	And if you look at its current level of about 8.5 percent, that kind of number has historically been associated with an unemployment rate of 5.3 or 5.4 percent.	13	1
201510	2342	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	That is higher than the current 5.1 percent, but it suggests that, at least on this broadest definition of underemployment, the unemployment rate is only understating the degree of slack by about 0.3 percentage point.	13	1
201510	2343	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	If you narrow it down to U-5, the difference is only about 0.1 percentage point.	13	1
201510	2344	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	So at least based on measures of people who actually say they want a job, there really isn’t a lot more slack out there.	13	1
201510	2345	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	There’s somewhat more slack, but not a lot more slack than the U-3 measure.	13	1
201510	2346	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Turning to price inflation, I expect that overshooting full employment next year will offset some of the transitory disinflationary pressures associated with the pass-through of lower import and energy prices into core prices.	13	1
201510	2347	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	This, along with the waning effects of these price shocks, will help speed a return to our 2 percent target.	13	1
201510	2348	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Still, I expect inflation to remain below our target for some time.	13	1
201510	2349	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	The natural question is, is the fact that inflation remains low an indication that the economy remains well below capacity—meaning there is actually more slack out there?	13	1
201510	2350	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Analysis by my staff suggests caution on this front.	13	1
201510	2351	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Core PCE inflation has been pushed down by a decline in health services inflation driven by public payment adjustments, including limits on Medicare payment growth associated with the Affordable Care Act and the “doc fix” bill.	13	1
201510	2352	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	These public payment adjustments are especially important because private payments tend to follow Medicare’s lead.	13	1
201510	2353	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Of course, the PCE price index that we follow is actually based on these PPI measures of how much the government and other payers pay hospitals, doctors, and other providers.	13	1
201510	2354	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Because the PCE price index places a relatively large weight on health-care prices, the unusually weak health-care inflation may obscure evidence of a cyclical rebound in underlying inflation.	13	1
201510	2355	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Therefore, the low rate of core PCE inflation cannot be taken fully onboard as an indication that there’s still substantial slack in the economy.	13	1
201510	2356	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	All in all, these findings make a good case for looking to other inflation indicators, such as the Dallas Fed trimmed mean.	13	1
201510	2357	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	The trimmed mean is currently particularly attractive because it removes some of the legislative and administrative price changes in the health-care sector.	13	1
201510	2358	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	All told, while inflation remains low, this is the result of a confluence of transitory and special factors.	13	1
201510	2359	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	It’s not indicative of the performance of the broad economy, which remains solid.	13	1
201510	2360	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	As slack continues to diminish and the influences of the strong dollar, low energy prices, and the effects of these legislative changes to health-care prices fade, I’m confident that inflation will move back to our 2 percent target.	13	1
201510	2361	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you.	13	1
201510	2362	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1
201510	2363	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Brainard.	13	1
201510	2364	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201510	2365	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Economic data since September have been mixed, but on balance they suggest little difference in my assessment from when we last met.	13	1
201510	2366	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Although some of the heightened risk aversion that led to declines in the prices of risky assets over the summer has faded, the key risk associated with the outlook—the potential for weak foreign demand to weigh even more heavily on economic growth and inflation in the United States—is not materially different.	13	1
201510	2367	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Turning first to the labor market, measures of labor utilization suggest that there has been little change in labor market slack since September.	13	1
201510	2368	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The unemployment rate was unchanged last month while other indicators of slack were mixed:  The drop in the participation rate suggested no reduction in slack, and the decline in the share of involuntary part-time workers pointed to some further lessening.	13	1
201510	2369	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Both of these alternative measures, together with no sign of a pickup in wage growth, continue to suggest there may be labor slack remaining.	13	1
201510	2370	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	At the same time, the pace of job gains appears to have slowed somewhat in recent months.	13	1
201510	2371	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Nonfarm payroll employment increased 142,000 in September, little different from the currently published figure for August.	13	1
201510	2372	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	As a result, the average monthly change in the third quarter is somewhat below the pace of job gains in the first half of the year.	13	1
201510	2373	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The slowing in employment growth looks to coincide with a similar slowing in output growth.	13	1
201510	2374	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	After rising at a 2½ percent rate over the first half of the year, real GDP growth appears to have downshifted to a pace below 2 percent in the third quarter.	13	1
201510	2375	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	However, a decline in inventory investment, a factor that boosted activity in the first half, likely accounts for that reduction in GDP growth.	13	1
201510	2376	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Abstracting from this component of aggregate demand, which is characteristically volatile, final sales increased at close to a 2 percent pace in the first half of the year, and I’d expect a similar increase in the second half.	13	1
201510	2377	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	I’m encouraged that consumer spending appears to have increased at more than a 3 percent pace in the middle two quarters of the year, with auto sales rising strongly.	13	1
201510	2378	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Business fixed investment also looks to have moved higher over this period despite considerable drag generated by sharp declines in energy-related investment.	13	1
201510	2379	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The housing sector also continues to expand, though the pace is very gradual and activity remains well below what population growth would suggest.	13	1
201510	2380	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	And recent developments on Capitol Hill hold out hope that the debt limit, a key source of uncertainty, may be addressed in a broader deal that could lead to federal government spending that is slightly higher than what was earlier projected.	13	1
201510	2381	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	However, recent trade data, which will get updated tomorrow, suggest that net exports, after subtracting close to 1 percentage point from GDP growth in the first half of the year, held down growth again last quarter.	13	1
201510	2382	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Turning to inflation, measures of underlying inflation trends remain below our 2 percent target.	13	1
201510	2383	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The 12-month change in core PCE is estimated to have been 1.4 percent in September, while the 3-month change is estimated to have been about 1.5 percent, little different from the average pace of increase over the recovery.	13	1
201510	2384	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	On balance, these data suggest an underlying trend in inflation that is between 1¼ and perhaps 1¾ percent.	13	1
201510	2385	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Progress toward our goals will be heavily affected by the competing influences of relatively robust private demand in the United States, on the one hand, and weak foreign economic growth on the other.	13	1
201510	2386	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Despite extraordinary monetary stimulus in Japan and the euro area, growth remains very weak.	13	1
201510	2387	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Real GDP in Japan appears to be increasing at a rate barely above zero, while growth in the euro area is increasing at a relatively modest rate of between 1½ and 2 percent.	13	1
201510	2388	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Over much of the recovery, weakness in advanced economies has been offset by strong economic growth in emerging market economies, but this has changed.	13	1
201510	2389	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Trade- weighted real GDP growth in emerging market economies has slipped from an annual average of 4½ percent from 2009 to 2013 to 1 percent less than that last year and to an annual rate of only 2 percent in the first half of this year.	13	1
201510	2390	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	As a result, total foreign economic growth was 1½ percent in the first half of the year, the lowest two-quarter growth rate since the recession.	13	1
201510	2391	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Weak foreign demand pushes down the contribution to real GDP growth from net exports both directly and indirectly by putting upward pressure on the dollar.	13	1
201510	2392	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	In addition, concerns about the foreign outlook have at times led to a pullback by investors from risky assets and heightened worries of deflationary pressures.	13	1
201510	2393	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	A worsening global outlook has likely been responsible for much of the deceleration in employment and overall activity in the United States this year.	13	1
201510	2394	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Whether this downward pressure eases or intensifies is an open question.	13	1
201510	2395	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	The most recent news regarding emerging markets, particularly China, has been mixed.	13	1
201510	2396	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	One the one hand, real GDP in China is reported to have risen at an annual rate of more than 7 percent in the third quarter, consistent with an overall slowing trend but a considerably better outcome than some had feared.	13	1
201510	2397	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Moreover, estimates of employment have held up well, and services-sector output looks to have accelerated.	13	1
201510	2398	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	On the other hand, growth in industrial production remained depressed in September.	13	1
201510	2399	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	If this reflects a structural shift that persists, it suggests that downward pressures on commodity prices and commodity exporters are unlikely to ease measurably going forward.	13	1
201510	2400	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Chinese policymakers are currently pursuing several competing goals—transitioning to a more services- and consumer-oriented economy, pushing through difficult financial and economic reforms, and preventing a significant undershooting of economic growth—making their task tremendously complicated.	13	1
201510	2401	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Those complications are enhanced by the poor quality of their macroeconomic data and challenges in the communication of their policies.	13	1
201510	2402	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	That poses risks both to the upside and the downside.	13	1
201510	2403	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	It is possible that Chinese policymakers, in an attempt to minimize economic underperformance and the attendant political risks, will institute a full-scale fiscal stimulus, which would boost economic growth, with positive ramifications globally.	13	1
201510	2404	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Under this scenario, the US economy might move somewhat more rapidly toward our goals than is currently expected.	13	1
201510	2405	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	However, the most common method for Chinese policymakers to expand is to channel funds into local governments.	13	1
201510	2406	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	These policies have led to large amounts of wasteful investment and huge increases in leverage, and they could work against the longer-term structural reform agenda that the policymakers have laid out.	13	1
201510	2407	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	As a result, we may see continued hesitation to reemploy this method of stimulus.	13	1
201510	2408	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	In that case, slower economic growth would continue to pose risks to the downside, as financial stress would increase and confidence might erode.	13	1
201510	2409	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	It’s possible, in that scenario, that slowing in global growth continues or intensifies and that upward pressures on the dollar increase, weighing further on our economic growth and inflation.	13	1
201510	2410	2015-10-28	meeting	BRAINARD	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201510	2411	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1
201510	2412	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Vice Chairman.	13	1
201510	2413	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201510	2414	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	My views on the economy haven’t changed much since the September meeting.	13	1
201510	2415	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The good news is that domestic final sales still seem to be rising relatively briskly, and the rise appears to be broad based.	13	1
201510	2416	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Consumption, housing, and business fixed investment all seem to be growing at a respectable pace.	13	1
201510	2417	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The bad news, though, is that the economy may be downshifting to a somewhat slower overall growth pace.	13	1
201510	2418	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	As many people have mentioned, inventory drag is going to be a big factor in the weakness we see in the third quarter, and net trade is not only going to be a drag in the third quarter, it’s going to be a drag for many quarters to come.	13	1
201510	2419	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The improvement in labor market conditions, at least measured by the Board’s broad- based measures, stalled out in September, but it’s unclear to me whether this is a one-time blip or the start of a new phase in which payroll gains slow and the improvement in the labor market grinds to a halt.	13	1
201510	2420	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	It seems to me that labor market developments over the next two months are going to be very important in assessing whether the timing is right for liftoff in December.	13	1
201510	2421	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The key question for me is, will the slowdown in economic growth that I think we’re seeing lead to lower payroll gains, and will those lower payroll gains, in turn, lead to weaker consumption growth?	13	1
201510	2422	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	A number of people have postulated that consumption will stay strong regardless, but if you have a weaker economy leading to lower payroll gains, there’ll be less income, and I start to worry about how that dynamic will play out.	13	1
201510	2423	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I think the downside risks to the outlook arising from overseas developments have subsided.	13	1
201510	2424	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	It is noteworthy that we actually do see real GDP growth abroad finally accelerating in the third quarter, compared with the second quarter.	13	1
201510	2425	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	And although the level of market stress increased very sharply late this summer, I think it is quite significant that even though we really ratcheted up the degree of stress, nothing of significance seems to have broken in response to that stress.	13	1
201510	2426	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	This is important because the way that a situation like this gets out of hand, as we saw in 2008 and 2009, is that stress causes things to break, which creates more stress, and events start to unfold in a very bad way.	13	1
201510	2427	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I think the fact that equity prices in emerging markets have rebounded quite significantly in recent weeks is also significant.	13	1
201510	2428	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The big question I have with respect to that is, how much of that improvement is due to the fact that nothing is broken, which some people are taking some comfort from, and how much is due to the fact that there’s been a shift in expectations about our own policy rate path?	13	1
201510	2429	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	And, at this point, I just don’t know the answer to that.	13	1
201510	2430	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Nevertheless, it is a positive development.	13	1
201510	2431	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	On the inflation side, I think the news has been relatively constructive in the sense that the core rate of inflation has been broadly stable despite the downward impetus to commodity prices due to the behavior of the US dollar.	13	1
201510	2432	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Although we haven’t seen any meaningful uptick in nominal wage growth, I think the anecdotal reports are generally consistent with emerging pressures.	13	1
201510	2433	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	For example, you hear the story about the truck drivers almost everywhere at this point.	13	1
201510	2434	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	But we need to be careful about the inference that we take away from the fact that nominal wage growth has not picked up.	13	1
201510	2435	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I found the staff memo on compensation and labor market slack very helpful in terms of my own thinking.	13	1
201510	2436	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	And I agree that it’s certainly possible that the wage gains have been restrained because headline inflation has fallen and productivity growth has faltered, and that those two factors may be offsetting the effect of a labor market that is, in fact, closing in on full employment.	13	1
201510	2437	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	What I take away from that is, one, I’m pretty uncertain, and, two, as a consequence I wouldn’t be comfortable making a pickup in wages a necessary condition for liftoff.	13	1
201510	2438	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The pressure may already be there, but we’re just not seeing it in the aggregate data because the upward pressure has been offset by these other factors that are restraining nominal wage growth.	13	1
201510	2439	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The fly in the ointment on the inflation side, which people haven’t really commented on very much, is the persistent decline in inflation compensation.	13	1
201510	2440	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I understand why inflation compensation is coming down over the near term because of the weakness in oil prices and other commodity prices, but the persistent declines in the five-year, five-year-forward measures are more difficult to explain and, therefore, more troubling.	13	1
201510	2441	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Although I don’t have any reason to dispute the model results—that this is mostly about the liquidity risk premium and what people are willing to pay for inflation protection—I do have some anxiety that we’re putting a lot of weight on particular models to disentangle the sources of the decline in inflation compensation.	13	1
201510	2442	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	The fact that some of the models are constructed in such a way that they beg the conclusion and push that weight onto liquidity risk and the price of inflation protection makes me a bit more concerned.	13	1
201510	2443	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	So I wouldn’t want to dismiss the decline in five-year, five-year-forward inflation compensation altogether.	13	1
201510	2444	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I think what I need to see is economic data that make me comfortable that the economy is likely to continue to grow at an above-trend pace in 2016, that core inflation is flat or rising, and that inflation expectations are well anchored.	13	1
201510	2445	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	If I see that, I think we can definitely justify liftoff and probably justify a liftoff at the next meeting.	13	1
201510	2446	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I do think, though, that we need to talk about alternatives to that scenario, and I’m going to talk tomorrow a little bit about what should happen if the economy should fall meaningfully short of the baseline forecast.	13	1
201510	2447	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I do think we need a Plan B for monetary policy in case the economy disappoints, because if we don’t lift off in December, the next question for the Chair is going to be, what happens if the economy continues to weaken, and what are you actually prepared to do about it?	13	1
201510	2448	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	And I really don’t think we’ve had that conversation around the Committee table yet to a sufficient degree.	13	1
201510	2449	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I think this is very important, and I very much welcome your thoughts on that tomorrow.	13	1
201510	2450	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	I’m going to go at the very end, as I always do, but if you have anything to say earlier, I’d love to hear what you think we should do if the economy disappoints and we don’t lift off in December, because I think we need to have an answer.	13	1
201510	2451	2015-10-28	meeting	DUDLEY	Narayana, maybe you’ll have a few thoughts on that.	13	1

201510	2498	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Here I am going to be giving, I suppose, a countervailing story with regard to what President Williams described.	13	1
201510	2499	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Household employment fell dramatically during the recession.	13	1
201510	2500	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Recovery from that decline, even for prime-age workers, remains highly incomplete.	13	1
201510	2501	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The fraction of those aged 25 to 54 who have a job remains 3 percentage points below its pre-recession peak in early 2007.	13	1
201510	2502	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If we split this group more finely by age, the story remains the same:  If you look at those aged 25 to 34, 34 to 45, and 45 to 54, EPOP is down 3 percentage points.	13	1
201510	2503	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If you split it by sex, EPOP for men aged 25 to 54 is down by 3½ percentage points; for women in that age group, it is down by only 2½ percentage points.	13	1
201510	2504	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	You can split it by race: Among African Americans aged 25 to 54, EPOP is down by 3 percentage points compared with 2007.	13	1
201510	2505	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The bottom line is, however you want to slice the data, prime-age employment is about 3 percentage points lower than in 2007.	13	1
201510	2506	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Given what we know about labor supply elasticities for prime-age workers, it seems very challenging to rationalize this large decline as being due to structural changes in the labor markets.	13	1
201510	2507	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So we have some room for inflation, and this strong decline in prime-age employment that suggests that we have more room to work on that front as well.	13	1
201510	2508	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Let me turn, then, to inflation expectations.	13	1
201510	2509	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I have already noted, inflation has been low for years, is currently low, and is expected to remain low for years to come.	13	1
201510	2510	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Prime-age employment has been low for years, is currently low, and is expected to remain low for years to come.	13	1
201510	2511	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	To achieve our goals more rapidly, it would seem like we should be providing more accommodation.	13	1
201510	2512	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But the tilt of policy is very much toward a continuation of the gradual tightening cycle that the Committee began in May 2013, as opposed to providing an increase in accommodation, as seems appropriate given where we are.	13	1
201510	2513	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So there is a disconnect between our policy direction and our policy goals, and I think this disconnect creates a significant credibility risk.	13	1
201510	2514	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The public and the markets may come to believe that we will not achieve our stated longer-term objectives.	13	1
201510	2515	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And I don’t think of this credibility risk as being a theoretical one.	13	1
201510	2516	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As alternative A correctly states, the Federal Reserve’s measure of five-year, five-year-forward inflation breakevens is near or at historical lows.	13	1
201510	2517	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Alternative B is more cagey about this point, simply saying that it has slipped a little bit.	13	1
201510	2518	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So I would counsel the Committee to be more transparent about that in the statement.	13	1
201510	2519	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Then if you choose to ignore that information, that’s fine.	13	1
201510	2520	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But I think the facts of the matter are that inflation breakevens are near or at historical lows.	13	1
201510	2521	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Low breakevens are often dismissed as being due to a decline in liquidity premiums, risk premiums, or inflation expectations.	13	1
201510	2522	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In terms of liquidity premiums, we see the same low measures of longer-term inflation expectations in zero-coupon inflation swaps.	13	1
201510	2523	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And this was not always true.	13	1
201510	2524	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If you go back to the end of 2008, zero-coupon inflation swaps did not fall anywhere near as much as inflation breakevens or TIPS did.	13	1
201510	2525	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So I would say that there is little evidence that liquidity premiums are the key driver of the low breakevens in 2015.	13	1
201510	2526	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In terms of risk premiums, I thought the Tealbook box was very interesting in terms of how you might try to decompose between risk premiums and expectations and how complicated that is.	13	1
201510	2527	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But, as I have argued before, our point of view in Minneapolis is that you should think about risk premiums as being highly informative from an economic point of view.	13	1
201510	2528	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Investors are increasingly betting that inflation will be unduly low at exactly the same time that economic activity is low.	13	1
201510	2529	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Put another way, investors are increasingly betting that the FOMC will be willing to miss both of its mandates at the same time, in the same direction, over the longer term.	13	1
201510	2530	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	These bets aren’t crazy.	13	1
201510	2531	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	They seem entirely reasonable given the persistent disconnect between our actions and our putative objectives.	13	1
201510	2532	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I somewhat share President Bullard’s concerns that the markets, at least, are putting more weight on our being in a low-inflation, low-economic-activity outcome.	13	1
201510	2533	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Where I disagree with him is that I don’t think it is because we have committed to a low interest rate policy.	13	1
201510	2534	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In fact, the decline in inflation expectations that you see in market-based measures really set in in early 2013.	13	1
201510	2535	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That is associated with the Committee beginning its gradual tightening cycle, not with committing to low interest rates.	13	1
201510	2536	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Madam Chair, I will summarize briefly.	13	1
201510	2537	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Inflation is low and is expected to remain low for years.	13	1
201510	2538	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Prime-age employment is low and is expected to remain low for years.	13	1
201510	2539	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And yet the tightening cycle and talk about it continues, and that creates a troubling belief in markets that we may be willing to fail in both our mandates for years to come.	13	1
201510	2540	2015-10-28	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I will build on this theme in our discussion of policy tomorrow when, ironically enough, I get to lead off.	13	1

201510	2575	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thanks to everyone for an interesting and thoughtful round of comments.	13	1
201510	2576	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	I know it’s been a long day, but with your permission, I would like to add some comments of my own on the outlook, and in the process anticipate tomorrow’s policy discussion.	13	1
201510	2577	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	I want to start by reviewing how, in my view, the economic situation has evolved over the past few months.	13	1
201510	2578	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	At the time of our July meeting, my expectation was that if the federal funds rate remained near zero, monthly payroll gains would remain close to 200,000, and the unemployment rate would steadily decline.	13	1
201510	2579	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Delaying liftoff until the transitory effects of low oil prices and dollar appreciation on 12-month inflation had totally dissipated—something that looked quite unlikely before spring of 2016—risked a situation in which the unemployment rate might well hit 4½ percent by the middle of next year, with further declines quite likely.	13	1
201510	2580	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	I worried that excessive pressure on resources could well develop under these conditions, eventually generating an unwelcome rise in inflation or inappropriate risk-taking in financial markets.	13	1
201510	2581	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	To avoid ending up in a situation in which we might need to tighten policy abruptly— something that could easily disrupt the economy—I anticipated that it would be appropriate to start tightening at our September meeting.	13	1
201510	2582	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	In September, of course, we decided to keep the funds rate unchanged, in large part because of concerns about global financial and economic conditions and the associated potential for adverse spillovers to the US economy.	13	1
201510	2583	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Although downside risks surely remain, I would say that developments on the global economic front have been largely reassuring.	13	1
201510	2584	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	That said, incoming information, primarily pertaining to the labor market, has for me served to cloud the outlook, with the consequence that I am less confident now than in July that resource utilization will in fact continue rising at the solid pace I anticipated then.	13	1
201510	2585	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Now, it is entirely possible that no change in my midsummer assessment of the modal economic outlook and its policy implications is warranted.	13	1
201510	2586	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	If that’s true, I would see lifting off at the next meeting as entirely appropriate.	13	1
201510	2587	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	But I think it would be wise for us to collect some additional information before making up our minds on December.	13	1
201510	2588	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	With respect to the pace of economic growth, I consider an underlying trend that is expected to be materially in excess of the growth of potential to be a prerequisite for tightening.	13	1
201510	2589	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Third-quarter real GDP growth looks likely to fall short of that trend, but a big swing in the contribution from inventory investment is a significant but temporary restraining factor.	13	1
201510	2590	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	And although net exports will likely continue to exert a sizable drag on GDP growth, the pace of final domestic demand seems pretty solid to me based on incoming data.	13	1
201510	2591	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	I expect, in line with the Tealbook, that economic growth will run around 2 percent or a bit more in Q4 and beyond.	13	1
201510	2592	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	But we will know a good deal more by the time of the December meeting.	13	1
201510	2593	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	What worries me more are the weaker readings we have received on the labor market.	13	1
201510	2594	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Monthly payroll gains were appreciably smaller in the past two labor reports, the unemployment rate stopped falling, and the labor force participation rate declined appreciably.	13	1
201510	2595	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	In addition, recent JOLTS data on job openings, quits, and hiring have leveled out or weakened.	13	1
201510	2596	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	As President Mester and the Vice Chairman noted, our staff’s labor market conditions index was flat in September, suggesting that the labor market just treaded water—although I did take on board President Mester’s interesting comments, and I think we should pursue those issues with the staff.	13	1
201510	2597	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	I will do that.	13	1
201510	2598	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	It is unclear whether this apparent slowdown is merely transitory or whether it represents something more persistent, such as a pickup in productivity growth or a decline in overall economic growth momentum.	13	1
201510	2599	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Fortunately, with two more labor reports in hand, we should have a better sense of what is going on by December.	13	1
201510	2600	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	If payroll growth does pick up to 180,000 per month, as the staff projects, and data on spending suggests growth at least in line with the Tealbook, I would be more confident that my late summer assessment remains broadly correct.	13	1
201510	2601	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Specifically, liftoff would be appropriate in order to slow the pace of expansion to a more prudent rate.	13	1
201510	2602	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	But if payroll gains remain around 140,000, I would see the case for an immediate tightening as at least less compelling.	13	1
201510	2603	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Job growth at this pace would imply a slower take-up of slack in coming months.	13	1
201510	2604	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	We have had a good discussion about labor force participation.	13	1
201510	2605	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	But if the staff is correct that the participation rate is likely to remain stable through much of next year, and the ratio of household employment to payroll employment is likely to edge down, then payroll gains of this magnitude would also be consistent with little or no change in the unemployment rate in 2016.	13	1
201510	2606	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	An important consideration in deciding when to begin tightening is the amount of slack that remains in the labor market.	13	1
201510	2607	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	On this issue, my assessment has changed slightly since late July.	13	1
201510	2608	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	As I noted at our previous meeting, the disappointing news on wages was an important factor in leading me to revise down my estimate of the longer-run unemployment rate in my September SEP submission to 4.8 percent.	13	1
201510	2609	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	This revision still seems appropriate to me.	13	1
201510	2610	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Not only have the wage data remained disappointing, but the recent memo by the Board staff confirms that wage data are informative about slack.	13	1
201510	2611	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Interestingly, the state-space model used to examine this issue yields an estimate of the current natural rate of unemployment equal to 4½ percent, conditional on assuming, as I do, that longer-run inflation expectations are currently anchored at 2 percent.	13	1
201510	2612	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	If that estimate is accurate, then we are even further away from full employment than I previously thought.	13	1
201510	2613	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Now, I readily admit that all estimates of slack are imprecise, and that confidence intervals are sufficiently wide to include the possibility that we are already effectively back to full employment, on this metric.	13	1
201510	2614	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	But because at least one point estimate puts the current unemployment gap at more than ½ percentage point, and because involuntary part- time employment remains elevated while the labor force participation rate remains below the staff’s estimated trend, I think we still have a ways to go before we can confidently assert that we have fulfilled our employment mandate.	13	1
201510	2615	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Furthermore, allowing the unemployment rate to move lower for a time may be necessary for us to achieve our inflation mandate within a reasonable period of time.	13	1
201510	2616	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Looking beyond the near term, one critical factor that will influence the future pace of tightening will be the speed and extent to which the underlying strength of the economy improves over time.	13	1
201510	2617	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	As I noted during the r* session, I anticipate that the equilibrium real rate will move up somewhat over the next few years as various forces restraining growth gradually abate.	13	1
201510	2618	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	But this is only a forecast, and the outlook is highly uncertain.	13	1
201510	2619	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	In the face of such uncertainty, we will need to proceed cautiously in adjusting the stance of policy to verify, as time passes, that higher interest rates are in fact appropriate.	13	1
201510	2620	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	The staff memo on the policy implications of r* uncertainty supports this conclusion.	13	1
201510	2621	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	The memo demonstrates that when estimates of r* are uncertain, optimal monetary policy markedly attenuates the response of the funds rate to signs that the natural rate is rising, as long as the risk is high that adverse shocks could cause the zero lower bound to bind in the near future.	13	1
201510	2622	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	And, unfortunately, that is a risk we are likely to face for some time.	13	1
201510	2623	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	In addition, the staff finds that r* uncertainty increases the weight on inflation and optimized policy rules—a result I interpret, in view of how low inflation is at present, as strengthening the case for gradualist policy.	13	1
201510	2624	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Finally, on inflation, I don’t have much to add to what others and the Tealbook have said.	13	1
201510	2625	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	The incoming data have come in more or less as expected.	13	1
201510	2626	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	My outlook remains unchanged.	13	1
201510	2627	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	On a 12-month basis, I anticipate that PCE inflation should move up markedly over the course of next year, as the transitory effects of low energy prices and dollar appreciation fade.	13	1
201510	2628	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Thereafter, I expect inflation will gradually complete its return to 2 percent in the context of a labor market in which the unemployment rate modestly undershoots its longer-run level for a time.	13	1
201510	2629	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	That is, the Phillips curve, in my view, plays a role in helping us achieve our inflation goal.	13	1
201510	2630	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	I think that completes our economic go-round.	13	1

201510	2657	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	Let’s get started on monetary policy, and I’ll turn to Thomas for his briefing.	21	2
201510	2658	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Thank you, Madam Chair.	21	2
201510	2659	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	I will be referring to the handout labeled “Material for Briefing on Monetary Policy Alternatives.”	21	2
201510	2660	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	In light of the discussion you had about different measures of the equilibrium real interest rate and their implications for the conduct of monetary policy, I thought it might be useful to return to an analysis that I presented at the March meeting, which considered what your SEP submissions implied for the path, over coming years, of one particular concept of the equilibrium real rate.	21	2
201510	2661	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	To set the stage, the upper-left panel shows a noteworthy aspect of the median paths for the unemployment gap, core inflation, and the real federal funds rate in the September SEP:  The median of your projections of the real federal funds rate rises by nearly 3 percentage points over the next three years, while the medians of your projections of the unemployment gap and core inflation change very little.	21	2
201510	2662	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	As summarized in the upper-right panel, I use an IS equation that relates the unemployment gap (u minus u*), as a measure of the cyclical position of the economy, to the real rate gap, defined as the deviation of the real federal funds rate (r) from a time-varying equilibrium level (r*).	21	2
201510	2663	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	This definition of the equilibrium real rate has the property that, if the actual real rate were kept at its equilibrium level, over time the unemployment gap would close.	21	2
201510	2664	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Thus, in this set-up, the equilibrium real rate is a medium-run concept of what Chris Gust called the neutral policy rate, which varies over time in response to shocks that cause persistent shifts in the position of the IS curve.	21	2
201510	2665	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	The coefficients on the lagged unemployment gap and the lagged real rate gap in this equation are estimated using historical data for the fourth quarter of each year, so as to be consistent with the data that you provide in your SEPs, and using the staff’s historical estimates for u* and estimates for r* taken from the Laubach-Williams model.	21	2
201510	2666	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	With the estimated coefficients in hand, I then insert each participant’s projected values for the unemployment rate and the real federal funds rate, as well as his or her estimate of the longer-run normal unemployment rate, and solve the equation for the implied values of the time-varying r* at the end of each year over the forecast horizon.	21	2
201510	2667	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	The implied values for each participant’s projection are shown as the blue dots in the middle-left panel.	21	2
201510	2668	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Reflecting the diversity of your views about the economic outlook and appropriate monetary policy, these r* estimates are fairly dispersed.	21	2
201510	2669	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	The median (shown by the red line) is close to zero in both 2015 and 2016, then rises to ½ percent by 2017 and to a little below 1 percent by 2018, gradually approaching the longer-run median estimate of 1½ percent.	21	2
201510	2670	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Your projected paths for the real federal funds rate continue to run well below your longer-run normal values for the next year or two.	21	2
201510	2671	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	If r* were at its longer-run value, the IS equation would predict substantially more rapid declines in the unemployment rate than those in your projections.	21	2
201510	2672	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	To reconcile your unemployment rate projections with your real rate projections, it must be the case that this concept of r* remains depressed in 2015 and 2016.	21	2
201510	2673	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	This analysis suggests that the relevant measure of policy accommodation is the real rate gap, defined as the difference at each point in time between the real federal funds rate and the time-varying equilibrium real rate.	21	2
201510	2674	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	The middle-right panel shows that, for most of you, your projections imply a moderate degree of accommodation at present, but that, by this measure, monetary policy will no longer be accommodative by the end of 2017 despite the fact that most of you project the federal funds rate to remain low by historical standards.	21	2
201510	2675	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Of course, the expectations of market participants regarding the funds rate path are also an important element of the transmission of your monetary policy decisions to medium- and longer-term interest rates.	21	2
201510	2676	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Your final two panels examine the degree to which the expectations of market participants are aligned with the SEP.	21	2
201510	2677	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	The dark- blue dots in the lower-left panel show that the median respondent to the Desk’s latest primary dealer survey projected a path for the federal funds rate somewhat below the median SEP path from September, the light-blue dots.	21	2
201510	2678	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Against this backdrop, it is striking that the expected path for the federal funds rate implied by OIS quotes, the red line, lies substantially below the survey medians; the gap between survey measures and market-based estimates of the federal funds rate path has been persistent and has progressively widened over the course of this year.	21	2
201510	2679	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	One potential explanation, noted in the lower-right panel, is that the investors whose views determine these prices have a substantially more pessimistic economic outlook than the economists employed by the primary dealers.	21	2
201510	2680	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	We do not have any evidence to assess this possibility.	21	2
201510	2681	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	A second explanation could be that investors perceive predominantly downside risks to the outlook:  The dealer survey represents expectations of the most likely, or modal, outcomes, whereas the path implied by OIS quotes reflects a mean expectation.	21	2
201510	2682	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	For example, the dealers continue to place a 1-in- 5 probability on the outcome that the federal funds rate will have to return to the zero lower bound within two years following liftoff.	21	2
201510	2683	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Finally, the gap might reflect sizable negative term premiums:  Investors might place substantial probability on adverse economic outcomes materializing over the next few years.	21	2
201510	2684	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	If these contracts, and Treasury securities at similar maturities, offer insurance against such outcomes, investors could be willing to accept a low or even negative expected return.	21	2
201510	2685	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Turning to the draft statements for this meeting:  Your second exhibit, alternatives A, B, and C offer different assessments of current conditions, the outlook, and progress toward the Committee’s stated criteria for beginning policy normalization.	21	2
201510	2686	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	As indicated in the bullets in the top panel of your second exhibit, paragraph 1 of alternative B would provide an assessment that, despite soft net exports, real GDP has been expanding at a moderate rate, supported recently by “solid” gains in household and business spending and further improvement in the housing sector.	21	2
201510	2687	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	The Committee’s overall assessment of labor indicators in paragraph 1 would continue to be that, despite some softer data recently, “on balance, ... underutilization of labor resources has diminished since early this year.”	21	2
201510	2688	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	And its assessment of recent inflation trends would be largely unchanged, although you will need to decide how to adjust the description of the change in measures of inflation compensation over the intermeeting period.	21	2
201510	2689	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	As of yesterday’s close, 5-to-10-year inflation compensation from TIPS had declined 10 basis points since the September meeting.	21	2
201510	2690	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	In summarizing the outlook, paragraph 2 would convey that the Committee again expects economic activity to expand at a moderate pace.	21	2
201510	2691	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	The Committee’s statement of its expectations that labor market indicators and inflation will move toward mandate-consistent levels over the medium term would be unchanged.	21	2
201510	2692	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	With regard to risks to the outlook, paragraph 2 drops the separate sentence on foreign developments that the Committee added in September and instead reports that, while the Committee continues to see the risks to the outlook as “nearly balanced,” it is monitoring “global economic and financial developments.”	21	2
201510	2693	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	These changes would signal that the Committee is now less concerned about the implications of developments abroad than was the case in September.	21	2
201510	2694	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	On the Committee’s criteria for increasing the target range for the federal funds rate, alternative B would convey that the Committee still needs to see “some further improvement in the labor market” and is not yet reasonably confident that “inflation will move back to its 2 percent objective over the medium term.”	21	2
201510	2695	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	However, in paragraph 3, the Committee would update its communications about the timing of the first increase in the target range for the federal funds rate.	21	2
201510	2696	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Rather than focusing on “how long to maintain” the current target range, the Committee would suggest that such a decision may be close by indicating that “in determining whether it will be appropriate to raise the target range later this year”—or, alternatively, “at its next meeting”—the Committee will, “based on incoming data,” assess realized and expected progress toward its objectives.	21	2
201510	2697	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Although this guidance would leave the Committee wide latitude at the December meeting, it would likely suggest to some market participants a higher likelihood of a December liftoff than they perceived in advance of this meeting.	21	2
201510	2698	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	In contrast, alternative A would cause investors to push out the most probable date of the first increase in the target range and would likely lead them to shift down their expectations of the path thereafter.	21	2
201510	2699	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Alternative A would provide a less sanguine reading of recent data on economic activity, citing the “drag” from inventory investment and net exports, and it would note the leveling out of the unemployment rate.	21	2
201510	2700	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	It would also indicate heightened concern about economic and financial developments abroad by stating that they have tilted the risks to the outlook for economic activity and the labor market to the downside.	21	2
201510	2701	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Alternative A would also convey much greater concern about the outlook for inflation by citing “subdued” rates of inflation, core inflation, and hourly compensation as well as the very low market- based measures of inflation compensation.	21	2
201510	2702	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Accordingly, alternative A would set aside the Committee’s current statement of its criteria for liftoff and, instead, state that “if incoming information does not soon indicate that inflation is beginning to move back toward 2 percent, the Committee is prepared to use all tools necessary to return inflation to 2 percent within one to two years.”	21	2
201510	2703	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Alternative C would begin policy normalization by announcing an increase of 25 basis points in the target range for the federal funds rate, reflecting a confident assessment that current and expected economic conditions have met the Committee’s criteria for liftoff.	21	2
201510	2704	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Specifically, the Committee would report that recent labor market indicators, including ongoing job gains, show further improvement and confirm an appreciable reduction in underutilization of labor resources since early in the year.	21	2
201510	2705	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	It would state that with appropriate adjustments in the stance of monetary policy, labor market indicators are expected not just “to move toward,” but also to “reach,” mandate-consistent levels.	21	2
201510	2706	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	In addition, the Committee would upgrade its assessment of risks to the outlook for economic activity and the labor market to “balanced.”	21	2
201510	2707	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	As would the other alternatives, under alternative C the Committee would acknowledge that “inflation is anticipated to remain near its recent low level in the near term” and would note that “market-based measures of inflation compensation remain near the low end of the range seen in recent years.”	21	2
201510	2708	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	But it would state with greater assurance that the effects of declines in energy prices and in prices of non- energy commodities that are currently holding down inflation “will dissipate.”	21	2
201510	2709	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	And under alternative C the Committee would say that with continuing improvement in the labor market and stable longer-term inflation expectations, “the Committee is reasonably confident that inflation will rise to 2 percent over the medium term.”	21	2
201510	2710	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	The forward guidance about the expected path of the target range of the federal funds rate in today’s draft of alternative C is much the same as we presented in September.	21	2
201510	2711	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	It states that “the timing and size of future adjustments” will be determined by the Committee’s assessment of realized and expected “economic conditions relative to its objectives of maximum employment and 2 percent inflation.”	21	2
201510	2712	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Alternative C presents the option to retain, in paragraph 4, the indication that the Committee “will take a balanced approach” to pursuing its objectives.	21	2
201510	2713	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	Thank you, Madam Chair.	21	2
201510	2714	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	That completes my prepared remarks.	21	2
201510	2715	2015-10-28	meeting	LAUBACH	I will be happy to take questions.	21	2

201510	2824	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	But what this phrase is telling people to do is to look at the couple of data points that are coming up.	22	2
201510	2825	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	Again, it’s a compromise.	22	2
201510	2826	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	It’s an attempt, as I think Thomas nicely explained, to have your cake and eat it, too.	22	2
201510	2827	2015-10-28	meeting	WILLIAMS	But that’s just the cost of this.	22	2
201510	2828	2015-10-28	meeting	YELLEN	President Rosengren.	22	2
201510	2829	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	I have two quick observations.	22	2
201510	2830	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	I do think, like Governor Brainard, that if we say “at its next meeting,” that indicates “probable,” not “possible.”	22	2
201510	2831	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	If we actually want “possible,” something like “soon” would be a fuzzed-up word.	22	2
201510	2832	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	I think we have to distinguish between what we would prefer and what we think we’re signaling.	22	2
201510	2833	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	I would prefer to say “at its next meeting,” because I’d rather make it probable.	22	2
201510	2834	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	But if the goal is to make it possible, I would probably just say “soon.”	22	2
201510	2835	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	Everybody can have a different view of the English connected with this, but I do think that, when you add a specificity of “at its next meeting,” it focuses the attention and the rest of the caveats get lost.	22	2
201510	2836	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	That’s just my own view of the English.	22	2

201510	2840	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	If part of the goal would be, in effect, a dovish first tightening—which is to say, we are tightening, but the path we’re taking is expected to be very gradual—I’m not sure the current version of C conveys that to the extent that I might want.	22	2
201510	2841	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	So, as you’re thinking about the next meeting, if it does seem to be appropriate, I’d suggest maybe rethinking that paragraph a little bit to more strongly indicate the path.	22	2

201510	2844	2015-10-28	meeting	ROSENGREN	If that’s the intent—to signal the path—then we might want to craft some language that does that.	22	2
